When Husband Acknowledges Pregnancy: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn on Testimony
Intra-Hanafi dispute on testimony in conditional divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the need for testimony when the husband acknowledges pregnancy in conditional divorce, and explain the reasoning behind each view.

Core Idea
Acknowledgment of pregnancy does not automatically prove birth—Sahibayn demand proof, Abu Hanifa trusts the wife.

This lesson explores a nuanced dispute within the Hanafi school regarding conditional divorce (talaq mu'allaq) linked to childbirth. When the husband has acknowledged the wife's pregnancy, does the divorce occur upon her mere claim of birth, or must she provide additional testimony? Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) diverge on this question, revealing deeper principles about acknowledgment, proof, and trust.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core legal issue
Determine the exact question at hand: whether the husband's acknowledgment of pregnancy eliminates the need for additional testimony when the wife claims she has given birth, triggering a conditional divorce.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and proof
Abu Hanifa holds that the divorce occurs without any testimony. His proof: acknowledging pregnancy implies acknowledging its necessary outcome (childbirth). Additionally, the wife is trusted (mu'tamanah) in matters relating to her body, so her statement is accepted.
The passage mentions two proofs: (1) iqrar of pregnancy includes what it necessitates, (2) the wife is entrusted, so her word is accepted in returning the trust.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and proof
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad require the testimony of a midwife (al-qabilah). Their proof: the wife's claim of breach (hanth) still requires proof; the midwife's testimony serves as that proof. Acknowledgment of pregnancy does not guarantee actual birth.
The passage is cut off, but the commentary indicates the Sahibayn's reasoning continues.
4
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: if the husband acknowledges pregnancy, under Abu Hanifa the wife's mere claim of birth triggers the divorce; under the Sahibayn, she must produce a midwife witness. This affects whether the divorce is effective immediately or delayed until testimony.
This difference can have significant implications for iddah, remarriage, etc.
5
Recognize the incompleteness of the passage
Note that the passage is cut off mid-sentence. The full hanafi response to the Sahibayn and any tarjih (preferred position) are not provided. Subsequent pages would complete the argument.
This incompleteness limits a definitive conclusion on the mufta bihi.

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on conditional divorce based on childbirth, now focusing on the scenario where the husband acknowledges the wife's pregnancy. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, if the husband acknowledges the pregnancy, the divorce occurs as soon as the wife claims birth, without needing any further testimony. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) require the testimony of a midwife to establish the birth, even if the husband acknowledged the pregnancy. The reasoning provided in the text (though cut off) includes: the wife's claim of breach still requires proof; the midwife's testimony is that proof. Additionally, acknowledging pregnancy implies acknowledgment of its natural outcome (childbirth). Also, the wife is trusted (mu'tamanah) to report the birth, so her statement is accepted in matters of trust (like returning a trust). The passage ends mid-sentence, likely explaining the Sahibayn's view further. This dispute turns on whether acknowledgment of pregnancy sufficiently establishes the occurrence of birth for the purpose of conditional divorce.

Key Terms
اقرار (iqrar): Acknowledgment or confession; here, the husband's admission that his wife is pregnant.
القابلة (al-qabilah): The midwife; a woman present at childbirth whose testimony is required by Sahibayn.
موتمنة (mu'tamanah): Trusted; the wife is considered trustworthy regarding matters of her body and childbirth.

Midwife Testimony in Conditional Talaq: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on evidentiary requirement for birth claim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the need for midwife testimony in conditional talaq, and explain the two rational proofs (dalil aqli) of Abu Hanifa.

Core Idea
The husband's acknowledgment of pregnancy implies acknowledgment of birth; the wife as trustee needs no witness.

This lesson examines a specific intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the evidentiary requirement when a wife claims she has given birth after the husband acknowledged her pregnancy and conditionally divorced her upon birth. The Sahibayn require a midwife's testimony; Imam Abu Hanifa argues it is unnecessary. Understanding the rational proofs clarifies the legal reasoning behind accepting the wife's sole claim.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario and the Hanafi positions
Establish the scenario: husband acknowledges pregnancy, then suspends divorce on birth; wife claims birth, husband denies. Determine the two positions: Sahibayn require midwife testimony; Abu Hanifa does not.
2
Examine the Sahibayn's proof
The Sahibayn argue that the wife is claiming the condition (birth) has occurred, and claims require proof. The testimony of one midwife suffices for matters of childbirth, as established in earlier fiqh rulings.
This is based on the principle that a single woman's testimony is accepted in women's affairs.
3
Analyze Abu Hanifa's first proof: acknowledgment of pregnancy is acknowledgment of birth
Abu Hanifa argues that by acknowledging pregnancy, the husband necessarily acknowledges its inevitable result—birth. Since he has already acknowledged birth, no further testimony is needed to prove it.
This is a rational inference (dalil aqli) based on causality.
4
Analyze Abu Hanifa's second proof: wife as trustee (amin)
By acknowledging pregnancy, the husband effectively declares the wife a trustee (amin) over the child as a trust (wadi'ah). The trustee's word is accepted in returning the trust, so her claim of birth suffices.
This analogy draws from the general fiqh principle that trustees are believed without witnesses.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
If no midwife is available, according to Abu Hanifa the divorce occurs immediately; according to the Sahibayn it does not. The mufta bihi in the Hanafi school is Abu Hanifa's view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب ثبوت النسب

صاحبین کے نزدیک دایہ کی گواہی شرط ہے کیونکہ عورت کے دعویٰ حث کیلئے حجت کا ہونا ضروری ہے اور ایک عورت ( دایہ ) کی شہادت اس باب میں حجت ہے۔ جیسا کہ ہم اوپر بیان کر چکے اور امام ابو حنیفہ کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ حاملہ ہونے کا اقرار اس چیز کا اقرار ہے جس کی طرف یہ پہنچے گا اور وہ ولادت ہے، اور اس لئے کہ شوہر نے عورت کے امانت دار ہونے کا اقرار کیا ہے۔ پس امانت واپس کرنے میں اس کا قول قبول ہوگا۔ تشریح مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ اگر شوہر اپنی بیوی کے حاملہ ہونے کا اقرار کر چکا پھر اس نے اس کی طلاق کو بچہ پیدا ہونے پر معلق کیا۔ پس عورت نے دعویٰ کیا کہ بچہ پیدا ہو گیا اور شوہر نے اس کی تکذیب کی۔ تو اس صورت میں امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک بغیر شہادت کے یہ عورت مطلقہ ہو جائے گی اور صاحبین کے نزدیک وقوع طلاق کیلئے دایہ کی شہادت شرط ہے۔

صاحبین کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ عورت نے اس بات کا دعویٰ کیا کہ میرا شوہر اپنی عبارت 'اذ ولدت ولداً فانت طالق' میں حانث ہو گیا اور شوہر کے حانث ہونے کو ثابت کرنے کیلئے ایک عورت (دایہ) کی شہادت کافی ہے جیسا کہ اوپر والے مسئلہ میں گذر چکا۔ اسلئے ہم نے کہا کہ ایک عورت ( دایہ ) کی شہادت شرط ہے۔

حضرت امام صاحب رحمۃ اللہ علیہ کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ شوہر کی جانب سے اپنی بیوی کے حاملہ ہونے کا اقرار در حقیقت نتیجہ عمل یعنی ولادت کا اقرار کرنا ہے اور جب شوہر کی طرف سے ولادت کا اقرار پایا گیا تو شہادت کی ضرورت باقی نہ رہی اس وجہ سے کہا گیا ہے کہ بغیر شہادت کے ولادت ثابت ہوگی اور طلاق واقع ہو جائے گی۔

دوسری دلیل یہ ہے کہ جب شوہر نے اس بات کا اقرار کیا کہ میری بیوی حاملہ ہے۔ تو گویا اس نے اس بات کا اقرار کیا کہ وہ امانت دار ہے۔ یعنی اسکے پیٹ میں میرا بچہ ودیعت ہے اور امانت واپس کرنے میں امین کا قول معتبر ہوتا ہے۔ اسلئے یہاں بھی امانت واپس کرنے یعنی بچہ جننے میں امین یعنی عورت کا قول قبول ہوگا ۔ شہادت وغیرہ کی ضرورت نہیں۔
Chapter on Establishing Lineage. According to the Sahibayn, the testimony of a midwife is a condition because for the woman's claim to be valid, evidence is necessary, and the testimony of one woman (midwife) is evidence in this chapter, as we have explained above. And Imam Abu Hanifa's proof is that the acknowledgment of pregnancy is an acknowledgment of that which it leads to, which is birth, and because the husband acknowledged that the woman is a trustee. So in returning the trust, her statement is accepted. Explanation of the issue: If the husband acknowledged that his wife is pregnant, then he suspended her divorce on the birth of the child. Then the woman claimed that the child was born and the husband denied it. In this case, according to Imam Abu Hanifa, without testimony, this woman becomes divorced, and according to the Sahibayn, for the occurrence of divorce, the testimony of a midwife is a condition. The proof of the Sahibayn is that the woman claimed that her husband became liable (hanith) in his expression 'when you give birth to a child, you are divorced', and to prove the husband's liability, the testimony of one woman (midwife) is sufficient, as has passed in the earlier issue. Therefore we said that the testimony of one woman (midwife) is a condition. The proof of Imam Abu Hanifa is that the husband's acknowledgment of his wife's pregnancy is in reality an acknowledgment of the result, i.e., birth, and when the husband's acknowledgment of birth is established, the need for testimony remains no more; for this reason it is said that without testimony, birth is established and divorce occurs. The second proof is that when the husband acknowledged that his wife is pregnant, it is as if he acknowledged that she is a trustee, i.e., his child is deposited in her womb, and in returning a trust, the statement of the trustee is reliable. Therefore here too, in returning the trust i.e., giving birth to the child, the statement of the trustee, i.e., the woman, will be accepted; testimony etc. is not needed.
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) regarding whether the testimony of a midwife is required when a woman claims she has given birth, after the husband acknowledged pregnancy and conditionally divorced her upon birth. The passage type is an intra-Hanafi dispute with reasoning. The Sahibayn argue that the woman's claim that the condition of divorce occurred requires proof, and the testimony of a single midwife suffices as evidence, analogous to earlier rulings. Imam Abu Hanifa counters with two rational proofs: first, the husband's acknowledgment of pregnancy necessarily entails acknowledgment of its result, namely birth, so no further testimony is needed. Second, by acknowledging pregnancy, the husband effectively declares the woman a trustee (amin) over the child as a trust (wadi'ah), and in returning a trust, the trustee's word is accepted without witnesses. The practical consequence (thamarah) is immediate divorce without any witness according to Abu Hanifa, while the Sahibayn require at least one midwife.

Key Terms
دایہ (dāyah): midwife; a woman who assists in childbirth.
شہادت (shahādah): testimony; evidence given by witnesses.
امانت (amānah): trust; something entrusted to another for safekeeping.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, what is the reason that the wife's claim of birth is accepted without midwife testimony?
A) Because the husband's prior acknowledgment of pregnancy implies acknowledgment of birth, and the wife is a trustee.
B) Because the wife is the legal guardian of the child.
C) Because two male witnesses are present.
D) Because the husband's denial is invalid.

Key Takeaway
In conditional talaq, the wife's claim of birth suffices without testimony when the husband has acknowledged pregnancy, due to implied acknowledgment and the wife's status as trustee.

Mother's Custody Right Returns Upon Divorce
States rule of custody return after marriage ends

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition under which a mother's custody right returns after being lost due to marriage to a non-mahram, and apply it to a scenario.

Core Idea
A mother's custody right is suspended by marriage to a stranger, but returns immediately when that marriage ends.

This passage clarifies that a mother's right to custody (haqq al-tarbiyah) is not permanently lost if she marries a non-mahram man. Rather, it is suspended during that marriage. When the marriage ends by divorce or death, the right automatically returns, restoring her priority over other relatives such as the maternal grandmother.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the mother's marriage to a non-mahram. The ruling is that this marriage suspends her custody right.
This suspension is not permanent; it is conditional on the marriage continuing.
2
Identify the condition for return
When the marital relationship (zawjiyyah) ends—by divorce or death—the impediment is removed, and the custody right returns.
The text uses the phrase 'زوجیت دور ہو جائے' (the marital relationship ceases).
3
Apply the rule to the example
In the example: mother marries stranger → custody goes to maternal grandmother → husband divorces mother → mother's right returns and becomes prior to grandmother's.
The example illustrates the automatic nature of the return.
4
Note the reasoning
The reasoning is that the preventing factor (marriage to a stranger) is removed, so the original right resumes.
The text says 'جو چیز روکنے والی تھی، وہ جاتی رہی' (the thing that was preventing is gone).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
یونکہ جدا ہوا اور نانا اس بچے کے باپ کے قائم مقام ہے۔ ہنوز اس کی شفقت باقی رہے گی ۔ یہی حال ہر ایسے قوم کا ہے جو اس بچہ کا کے بندا گی۔ یہ کا نرم ہو، کیونکہ قرابت تقریبہ کی صرف نظر کرتے ہوئے شفقت باقی رہے گی۔ اور جس عورت کا حق پرورش کسی اجنبی مرد سے نکاح کی وجہ سے زائل ہو گیا تو جب بھی ان دونوں میں زوجیت دور ہو جائے تو اس کا حق پرورش واپس آوے گا۔ کیونکہ جو چیز روکنے والی تھی ، وہ جاتی رہیں ۔ مثلا بچے کی ماں سب سے زیادہ حقدار ہے۔ لیکن اس نے کسی اجنبی مرد سے نکاح کر لیا حتیٰ کہ ان کا حق زائل ہو [؟] [؟] پس پرس نانی زیادہ حقدار ہوگی ۔ لوک نانی نے اس کو اپنی پرورش میں لے لیا ۔ پھر چند روز کے بعد اس کی ماں کے شوہر نے طلاق دے دی ۔ غرض یہ کہ نکاح زائل ہو گیا۔ تو ماں کا استحقاق بہ نسبت نانی کے مقدم ہو گیا۔
Because he separated, and the maternal grandfather stands in place of the child's father. Still his affection will remain. The same applies to every such relative who is a bond of the child. This is mild, because looking at proximate relationship, affection will remain. And whichever woman's right of custody is nullified due to marriage to a stranger, then whenever the marital relationship between them ends, her right of custody returns. Because the thing that was preventing is removed. For example, the child's mother is most entitled. But she married a stranger, so her right became nullified. ... then the maternal grandmother becomes more entitled. But the maternal grandmother took the child into her custody. Then after a few days, the child's mother's husband divorced her. In short, the marriage was nullified. So the mother's entitlement became prior to the maternal grandmother's.

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the return of a woman's custody right. The author explains that when a woman's custody right is lost because she married a non-mahram man (a stranger to the child), it is not permanently lost. If that marriage ends through divorce or death, her right to custody returns. The example given is of a mother whose right was lost to her own mother (the maternal grandmother) when she remarried. After her new husband divorced her, her right returned and became prior to the grandmother's. The reasoning is that the impediment (marriage to a stranger) has been removed. The opening lines refer to the maternal grandfather's affection continuing because he is in place of the father, establishing that affection is considered in custody.

Key Terms
حق پرورش (haqq al-tarbiyah): The right of custody or upbringing of a child
نکاح (nikah): Marriage, specifically a valid marriage contract
زوجیت (zawjiyyah): Marital relationship, the bond of marriage

The End of Mother's Custody: Independence Over Age
Defines the end of mother's custody for a boy: independence and age estimate

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi criterion for the end of a mother's custody over her son, explain the role of istighna' and istinja', and compare the Hanafi position with those of Imam Malik and Imam al-Shafi'i.

Core Idea
A boy's custody ends when he can independently care for himself, estimated at seven years.

In Hanafi law, a mother's right to custody is not indefinite; it ends when the child no longer needs her for basic self-care. This lesson explores the criterion of istighna' (independence) and the relied-upon age estimate of seven years. We will also see how this ruling differs from other schools and why the father's role in moral education becomes paramount after independence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its subject
The passage rules on the mother's (and grandmother's) entitlement to custody of a boy. The ruling is that she is entitled until the boy can independently perform basic tasks: eating, drinking, dressing, and cleaning himself.
The text mentions two formulations from al-Quduri (mentioning istinja') and al-Jami' al-Saghir (mentioning istighna'), but both refer to the same criterion.
2
Understand the criterion: istighna' and istinja'
The key concept is istighna' – the boy's independence in self-care. The ability to perform istinja' (cleaning after toilet) is considered the culminating indicator of independence.
Complete independence includes being able to eat, drink, dress, and clean alone.
3
Trace the age estimate and its basis
Imam al-Khassaf estimated this independence at seven years, based on common practice (ghalib). Abu Bakr al-Razi estimated nine years, but the fatwa is on seven.
The text says 'فتویٰ اسی پر ہے' – the fatwa is on the seven-year estimate.
4
Analyze the underlying wisdom (hikmah)
Once independent, the boy needs tahdhib (moral and social education) from male figures, and the father is better suited for this. Hence custody shifts to the father.
The shift is not arbitrary; it's based on the child's developmental needs.
5
Compare with other madhhabs
Imam Malik holds custody until the boy reaches puberty (ihtilam). Imam al-Shafi'i gives the child a choice between parents at age seven. The Hanafi view is intermediate.
The text presents these differences to highlight the Hanafi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والام والجدة احق بالغلام حتى ياكل وحده ويشرب وحده و يستنجى وحده وفي الجامع الصغير حتى يستغنى فياكل وحده ويشرب وحده ويلبس وحده والمعنى واحدلان تمام الاستغناء بالقدرة على الاستنجاء ووجهه انا اذا استغنى يحتاج الى التادب والتخلق بأداب الرجال واخلاقهم والاب اقدر على التثقيف والخصاف قدر الاستغناء بسبع سنين اعتبارا للغالب

ترجمہ: ماں اور نانی لڑکے کی زیادہ حقدار ہیں۔ یہاں تک کہ وہ اکیلا کھائے اور اکیلا پئے اور اکیلا کپڑے پہن لے اور اکیلا استنجا کر لے اور جامع صغیر میں ہے یہاں تک کہ لڑکا بے پروا ہو جائے کہ اکیلا کھائے، اور اکیلا پئے، اکیلا پہن لے اور معنی ( دونوں کے ) ایک ہی ہیں۔ کیونکہ پورا استغناء تو استنجا پر قادر ہونے سے ہوتا ہے۔ اور اس کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ جب وہ مستغنی ہو گیا تو اس کو مردوں کے آداب اور اخلاق سیکھنے کی حاجت ہے۔ اور ادب سکھلانے اور مہذب کرنے کی باپ کو زیادہ قدرت ہے۔ اور شیخ خصاف نے مستغنی ہو جانے کا اندازہ سات برس سے کیا ہے۔ کیونکہ غالب حالت یہی ہے۔

تشریح مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ ماں اور نانی بچہ کی زیادہ حقدار اس وقت تک کے لئے ہے جب کہ بچہ اکیلا کھانا کھانے لگے۔ اور اکیلا پئے اور اکیلا کپڑے پہن لے۔ اور اکیلا استنجا کر لے ۔ حضرت امام محمد نے جامع صغیر میں ذکر کیا کہ ماں اور نانی زیادہ حقدار لڑکے کی اس وقت تک ہیں جب تک کہ بچہ مستغنی ہو جائے کہ وہ اکیلا کھا سکے۔ اکیلا پی سکے اور اکیلا کپڑے پہن سکے۔ دونوں روایتوں میں یہ فرق ہے کہ قدوری میں استنجا مذکور ہے نہ کہ استغنا اور جامع صغیر میں اس کے برعکس ہے حاصل دونوں روایتوں کا ایک ہی ہے کیونکہ پورا استغناء اس وقت ہوگا جبکہ بچہ استنجے پر قادر ہو جائے اور استنجاء کرنے پر قدرت یہ ہے کہ ابتداء میں پائجامہ کھولنے پر قادر ہو اور فراغت کے بعد باندھنے پر قادر ہو جائے۔

اور استغنا کے ذکر کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ بچہ جب مستغنی ہو گیا تو وہ مردوں کے آداب و اخلاق سیکھنے کا محتاج ہوگا۔ اور بچہ کو مودب اور مہذب بنانے میں باپ کو زیادہ قدرت ہے۔ لہذا اب وہ ماں کی تربیت سے نکل کر باپ کی تربیت میں داخل ہوگا ۔ امام ابوبکر خصاف نے مستغنی ہو جانے کا اندازہ سات برس سے کیا ہے۔ کیونکہ بچہ سات سال کی عمر میں بالعموم ماں کی پرورش سے مستغنی ہو جاتا ہے۔ اور وہ اکیلا استنجا کرنے پر قادر ہو جاتا ہے ۔ فتویٰ اسی پر ہے۔ ابو بکر رازی نے نو برس سے اندازہ لگایا ہے۔ اور امام مالک کے نزدیک ماں لڑکے کی پرورش کرنے کی مستحق اس وقت تک ہوگی جب تک بچہ کو احتلام ہونے لگے۔ یعنی بالغ ہونے تک بچہ ماں کی پرورش میں رہے گا۔ اور امام شافعی فرماتے ہیں کہ بچہ جب سات برس کا ہو جائے تو اس کو اختیار دیا جائے کہ والدین میں سے جس کو اختیار کرے اس کو دے دیا جائے گا۔
The mother and grandmother are more entitled to the boy until he eats alone, drinks alone, and cleans himself alone. And in al-Jami' al-Saghir, until he becomes independent, eating alone, drinking alone, and dressing alone. The meaning is the same, because complete independence is through ability to clean oneself. The reason is that when he becomes independent, he needs to learn the manners and character of men, and the father is more capable of education and refinement. Al-Khassaf estimated independence at seven years, considering the common case. ...

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the duration of a mother's (or grandmother's) custody over a boy. The author explains that she is entitled until the boy can independently perform basic tasks: eating, drinking, dressing, and cleaning himself after using the toilet. The text cites two versions: one in al-Quduri mentioning istinja', and one in al-Jami' al-Saghir mentioning istighna' (independence). Both are reconciled: istighna' includes the ability to do istinja'. The underlying reasoning is that once the boy is independent, he needs to learn male manners and discipline, for which the father is better suited. Imam al-Khassaf estimated this age as seven years, which is the relied-upon view. The passage also notes that Abu Bakr al-Razi estimated nine years, and then mentions the positions of Imam Malik (until puberty) and Imam al-Shafi'i (child chooses at seven).

Key Terms
استغناء (istighna'): Independence of the child in basic self-care
استنجاء (istinja'): Cleaning oneself after using the toilet
تہذیب (tahdhib): Moral education and refinement

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Nine years
D) Until puberty

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school determines the end of a mother's custody over a son by his independence (istighna'), not by a fixed age; the seven-year estimate by al-Khassaf is the mufta bihi for practical application.

Mother's Custody: When Does It End?
Ruling on duration of mother's custody with rational justification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on when a mother's custody of a boy ends and explain the rational basis for the ruling.

Core Idea
Mother's custody ends when the boy can independently care for himself, typically estimated at seven years.

The Hanafi school gives the mother and maternal grandmother the primary right to custody of a young boy. But this right is not indefinite. In this passage, we learn the precise condition that marks the end of that right: the boy's ability to perform basic self-care tasks alone. We also explore the legal reasoning behind this rule and how it compares to other schools.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its duration
The mother and grandmother have the primary right (ahaqq) to custody of the boy until he reaches a specific stage of independence.
The text states 'until he eats alone, drinks alone, and performs istinja alone'.
2
Understand the condition of istighna (independence)
The boy must be able to eat, drink, dress, and perform istinja without help. The condition is not a fixed age but actual capability.
Imam Muhammad's expression 'yastaghni' (becomes independent) includes these tasks.
3
Analyze the rational basis (istihsan)
Once the boy is independent, he needs to learn male manners and discipline, which the father is better suited to teach. So custody transfers to the father.
This is a rational consideration overriding a possible analogy that would keep the mother as custodian.
4
Examine intra-Hanafi differences
Quduri explicitly mentions istinja; Jami' al-Saghir mentions 'independence'. They are reconciled: independence includes istinja. Khassaf estimates seven years as the typical age.
Al-Marghinani prefers Khassaf's estimate; fatwa is on seven years.
5
Compare with other madhhabs
Malik keeps custody until puberty; Shafi'i gives the child choice at seven. The Hanafi position is based on capability and paternal discipline.
The thamarah al-khilaf: practical difference in when the father assumes custody.

Source Text

اور استغنا کے ذکر کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ بچہ جب مستغنی ہو گیا تو وہ مردوں کے آداب و اخلاق سیکھنے کا محتاج ہوگا۔ اور بچہ کو مودب اور مہذب بنانے میں باپ کو زیادہ قدرت ہے۔ لہذا اب وہ ماں کی تربیت سے نکل کر باپ کی تربیت میں داخل ہوگا ۔ امام ابوبکر حصاف نے مستغنی ہو جانے کا انداز ہ سات برس کے ساتھ کیا ہے۔ کیونکہ بچہ سات سال کی عمر میں بالعموم ماں کی پرورش سے مستغنی ہو جاتا ہے۔ اور وہ اکیلا استنجا کرنے پر قادر ہو جاتا ہے ۔ فتویٰ اسی پر ہے۔ ابو بکر رازی نے نو برس کے ساتھ اندازہ لگایا ہے۔ اور امام مالک کے نزدیک ماں لڑکے کی پرورش کرنے کی مستحق اس وقت تک ہوگی کہ بچہ کو احتلام ہونے لگے۔ یعنی بالغ ہونے تک بچہ ماں کی پرورش میں رہے گا۔ اور امام شافعی فرماتے ہیں کہ بچہ جب سات برس کا ہو جائے تو اس کو اختیار دیا جائے کہ والدین میں سے جس کو اختیار کرے اس کو دیدیا جائے گا۔
And the mother and grandmother have more right to the boy until he eats alone and drinks alone and performs istinja alone, and in al-Jami' al-Saghir until he becomes independent so he eats alone and drinks alone and wears clothes alone, and the meaning is one because complete independence is by ability to perform istinja. And its justification is that when he becomes independent, he needs to be disciplined and adopt the manners and ethics of men, and the father is more capable of training. And Khassaf estimated independence at seven years, taking the dominant case as basis. Translation: Mother and grandmother have more right to the boy until he eats alone and drinks alone and wears alone and performs istinja alone. And in Jami' Saghir it is until the boy becomes independent so that he eats alone, drinks alone, wears alone, and the meaning (of both) is one. Because complete independence occurs from being able to perform istinja. And the reason for this is that when he becomes independent, he needs to learn the manners and ethics of men, and the father is more capable of teaching and disciplining. And Shaykh Khassaf estimated independence at seven years because the prevailing state is this. Explanation: The issue is that the mother and grandmother have more right to the child until the child begins to eat alone, drink alone, wear clothes alone, and perform istinja alone. Imam Muhammad mentioned in Jami' Saghir that the mother and grandmother have more right to the boy until the child becomes independent so that he can eat alone, drink alone, and wear clothes alone. The difference between the two narrations is that in Quduri istinja is mentioned and not independence, while in Jami' Saghir it is the opposite. The conclusion of both narrations is one because complete independence occurs when the child becomes able to perform istinja, and ability to perform istinja means that he is able to open his trouser at the beginning and fasten it after finishing. And the reason for mentioning independence is that when the child becomes independent, he needs to learn the manners and ethics of men. And the father is more capable in making the child disciplined and refined. Therefore, now he leaves the mother's training and enters the father's training. Imam Abu Bakr Khassaf estimated independence at seven years, because a child generally becomes independent from mother's care at age seven and becomes able to perform istinja alone. The fatwa is on this. Abu Bakr Razi estimated nine years. And according to Imam Malik, the mother is entitled to raise the boy until he experiences nocturnal emission, i.e., until puberty the boy will remain in the mother's care. And Imam Shafi'i says that when the boy reaches seven years, he should be given the choice to choose between his parents and will be given to the one he chooses.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the duration of the mother's (and grandmother's) right to custody (hadana) of a boy, with reasoning and intra-Hanafi differences. The matn states that the mother and grandmother have primary right to the boy until he can eat alone, drink alone, and perform istinja alone—essentially until he achieves basic self-care independence. In al-Jami' al-Saghir, Imam Muhammad expresses this as 'until he becomes independent' (yastaghni), which includes eating, drinking, and dressing alone. The author reconciles these two expressions by explaining that complete independence necessarily includes the ability to perform istinja (cleaning after relieving oneself), as that is a critical component of self-sufficiency. Once the boy reaches this stage, he needs to learn the manners and ethics of men (adab al-rijal), and the father is better equipped to teach him discipline and refinement. Imam Abu Bakr al-Khassaf estimated that this independence typically occurs at age seven, and this is the relied-upon position (mufta bihi). Abu Bakr al-Razi estimated nine years. The passage also notes the positions of other madhhabs: Imam Malik extends the mother's right until puberty (when the boy experiences nocturnal emission), and Imam al-Shafi'i gives the child a choice at age seven. The Hanafi position is based on practical capability rather than a fixed age, though the estimate of seven years serves as a guideline.

Key Terms
حضانة (hadana): Custody and nurture of a child, including physical care and upbringing.
استغناء (istighna): Independence in basic personal care: eating, drinking, dressing, and performing istinja alone.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when does the mother's right to custody of a boy end?
A) At exactly 7 years of age.
B) When the boy can eat, drink, dress, and perform istinja alone.
C) At puberty.
D) When the father demands.

Key Takeaway
The mother's right to custody ends not by a fixed age, but when the child achieves actual independence in basic self-care.

Mother and Grandmother's Custody of a Girl
Ruling on mother's custody of a girl until menstruation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the duration of mother's custody of a girl and explain the reasoning behind the differing riwayah from Imam Muhammad.

Core Idea
A mother's custody of her daughter ends at menstruation, but an earlier transfer at the age of desire is also reported from Imam Muhammad.

This passage from al-Hidayah addresses a critical question in family law: when does the mother's right to custody of her daughter transfer to the father? The Hanafi school uses both practical reasoning (maslaha) and an intra-madhhab riwaya to determine the boundary. We will examine the primary ruling from al-Quduri, the alternative view from Imam Muhammad, and the underlying rationale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling and Its Duration
Find the main ruling: the mother and grandmother have custody of a girl until she menstruates. Note that this applies to daughters, not sons (different ruling).
The age of menstruation varies but marks puberty (bulugh).
2
Examine the Primary Proof (Maslaha)
The text gives two justifications: (1) before puberty, the girl needs to learn feminine arts (like sewing, cooking) which women teach better; (2) after puberty, she needs protection and marriage oversight, which the father handles better. Both are public-interest (maslaha) arguments.
This is dalil aqli – rational reasoning, not a direct textual proof.
3
Note the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Imam Muhammad (via Hisham) reports that the girl should be handed over earlier, at the ‘age of desire’ (hadd al-shahwa). Recognize this as a legitimate ikhtilaf within the school.
The exact age of hadd al-shahwa is disputed among fuqaha (6–11 years).
4
Analyze the Opposing Dalil
Imam Muhammad's view is also based on maslaha: protection becomes necessary at a younger age, even before menstruation. Understand the reasoning but note that it is not the relied-upon position.
The opposing dalil sees risk of harm earlier; the main view sees menstruation as the natural boundary.
5
Determine the Mufta Bihi
The text indicates that the fatwa is on al-Quduri's position: until menstruation. The riwaya from Muhammad is noted but not preferred. This is the operative ruling for Hanafi practice.
Later Hanafi works often adopt the mainstream view for consistency.

And the mother and grandmother have more right to the girl until she menstruates, because after independence she needs to learn the manners of women, and the woman is more capable of that. And after puberty she needs to be protected and guarded, and the father is stronger and more guided in that. And from Muhammad [al-Shaybani] it is transmitted that she is handed over to the father when she reaches the age of desire (hadd al-shahwa) because the need for protection is realized. Translation: And the mother and grandmother are more entitled to raise the girl until she menstruates, because after becoming independent of care, she needs to learn the manners of women, such as sewing, weaving, cooking, washing clothes, and women are more capable of these than men. If the girl is handed over to the father, she would mix with men and lose her modesty, while modesty is the adornment of women. After puberty, she needs to be protected through marriage and guarded from adultery, and the father has more power and guidance for that. Therefore, after puberty, the father is more entitled. And Hisham narrated from Imam Muhammad that when the girl reaches the age of desire (hadd al-shahwa), she should be handed over to the father because the need for protection is realized. And the jurists differ regarding the age of desire: some say it is 11 years, Abu al-Buth said 9 years, and there are also views of 6, 7, and 8 years. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi ruling on the custody of a girl. According to al-Quduri, the mother and grandmother have primary right to the girl until she menstruates (hayd). The reasoning is twofold: before menstruation, after the girl becomes independent in basic care, she needs to learn feminine arts and manners (such as sewing, cooking, washing) which women teach better. If she were with the father, she might lose her modesty by mixing with men. After menstruation, she reaches puberty (bulugh) and needs to be protected and safeguarded, particularly by marriage (tahsin) and from zina. The father is more capable and guided in this role. Therefore, the father takes over. An additional riwaya from Imam Muhammad (narrated by Hisham) states that the girl is handed to the father when she reaches 'hadd al-shahwa' (age of desire), which is earlier than menstruation, indicating a need for protection. The text notes a difference of opinion among fuqaha on the exact age of hadd al-shahwa, with estimates ranging from 6 to 11 years, but no final tarjih is given.

Key Terms
حیض (hayd): Menstruation, which marks the end of the mother's custodial right over a girl.
حد الشهوة (hadd al-shahwa): The age at which a girl begins to experience sexual desire or becomes attractive; varies by opinion from 6 to 11 years.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) When she starts menstruating.
C) When she reaches the age of desire (hadd al-shahwa).
D) When she gets married.

Key Takeaway
The mother's custody lasts until menstruation, though an earlier transfer at the age of desire is reported but not relied upon.

Custody of a Girl by Other Female Relatives
Ruling on custody rights of non-mother/grandmother relatives

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the duration of custody rights for female relatives other than the mother and grandmother in the Hanafi school and explain the underlying rationale.

Core Idea
Other female relatives lose custody when the girl reaches desirability (hadd al-shahwa); mother's right lasts longer.

This passage from al-Hidayah clarifies the Hanafi ruling on how long female relatives other than the mother and grandmother may retain custody of a young girl. It distinguishes between the limit set by al-Quduri (hadd al-shahwa) and an expression from al-Jami' al-Saghir (tastaghni), and explains the rationale based on the ability to benefit from the child's service.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
Locate the explicit statement by al-Quduri: other relatives have custody until the girl reaches hadd al-shahwa (the age at which she becomes desirable to men).
This is the primary position presented.
2
Understand the alternative expression
Note the wording in al-Jami' al-Saghir: 'until she becomes independent' (tastaghni). This is reconciled with the first expression by the shared reason.
Both expressions point to the same end point in practice.
3
Analyze the reason (ta'lil)
The text explains that other relatives cannot take personal service from the girl or hire her out, so the purpose of custody is lost once she reaches a certain age. The mother and grandmother, however, can legally require service, so their custody continues longer.
The illa (effective cause) is the ability to benefit from the child's service.
4
Distinguish from mother's right
Contrast with the well-known rule that the mother retains custody of a girl until menstruation or marriage. Why does the mother's right extend beyond hadd al-shahwa? Because her entitlement is based on a different legal basis (shar'an).
The mother's custody is more robust; other relatives have a conditional and shorter right.
5
Apply the ruling
Consider a real scenario: after divorce, a maternal aunt has custody of a 9-year-old girl. At what age must she hand over custody? According to this ruling, custody ends when the girl reaches hadd al-shahwa, which may be assessed by physical signs or expert opinion.
In the absence of clear signs, judge may determine based on custom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و من سوى الام والجدة احق بالجارية حتى تبلغ حداتشتهى وفى الجامع الصغير حتى تستغنى لانها لا تقدر على استخد امها وهذا لا تواجرها للخدامة فلا يحصل المقصود بخلاف الام والجدة لقدر تهما عليه شرعا ترجمہ اور ماں اور نانی کے علاوہ (باقی عورتیں ) صغیرہ لڑکی کی پرورش میں اس حد تک حقدار ہیں کہ لڑکی مردوں کی خواہش کے قابل ہو جائے ۔ اور جامع صغیرہ میں ہے کہ وہ ( دوسرے کی مدد گاری سے مستغنی ہو جائے کیونکہ ماں اور نانی کے علاوہ کوئی عورت اس لڑکی سے خدمت لینے پر قادر نہیں ہے۔ اور اسی وجہ سے اس لڑکی کو خدمت کے لئے اجارہ اور نوکری پر نہیں دے سکتی تو مقصود حاصل نہ ہوگا۔ برخلاف ماں اور نانی کے۔ اس لئے کہ یہ دونوں شرعا خدمت لینے پر قادر ہیں۔ تشریح شیخ قدوری نے فرمایا کہ صغیرہ کو ماں اور نانی اور دادی کے علاوہ دوسری عورتوں کے پاس حد شہوت کو پہنچنے تک رکھا جاسکتا ہے
And besides the mother and grandmother, others have more right to the [custody of the] young girl until she reaches the stage of being desired (tubligh hadd al-shtiha), and in al-Jami' al-Saghir until she becomes independent (tastaghni), because she is not able to employ her for service, and she cannot hire her out for service, so the intended purpose is not achieved, contrary to the mother and grandmother, because they have the capacity for this legally. Translation: And besides the mother and grandmother, the remaining women are entitled to the custody of the minor girl until the girl becomes desirable to men. And in al-Jami' al-Saghir it is until she becomes independent of others' help, because no woman other than the mother and grandmother can take service from this girl, and she cannot hire her out for service, so the purpose is not achieved, unlike the mother and grandmother, because these two are legally capable of taking service. Explanation: Shaykh al-Quduri stated that the minor girl can be kept with women other than the mother, grandmother, and paternal grandmother until she reaches the age of desire (hadd al-shahwa).
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage addresses the custody rights of female relatives other than the mother and grandmother (such as aunts, sisters, etc.). According to al-Quduri, such relatives have the right to custody of a minor girl only until she becomes sexually desirable to men (tubligh hadd al-shtiha). In al-Jami' al-Saghir, it is expressed as 'until she becomes independent' (tastaghni), which is interpreted differently from the boy's case. The reason given is that women other than the mother and grandmother cannot effectively employ the girl for service or hire her out, so the benefit of custody is lost. In contrast, the mother and grandmother are legally permitted to take service from the child. Therefore, the right of these other relatives ends earlier than that of the mother and grandmother. The explanation from Sadr al-Shari'ah (via the text) specifies that 'until the age of desire' is the limit for others. The difference from the mother is clear: mother keeps until menstruation (for a girl), but other relatives lose custody once the girl reaches puberty in terms of desirability, which might be earlier.

Key Terms
حد الشهوة (hadd al-shahwa): The stage at which a girl becomes desirable to men, marking the end of custody rights for relatives other than mother/grandmother.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The custody right of female relatives other than the mother and grandmother ends at hadd al-shahwa, due to their inability to benefit from the child's service like the mother can.

Divorced Mother Taking Child Out of the City: Conditions and Limits
Ruling with proof on custody mobility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whether a divorced mother may take her child out of the city and identify the conditions that permit or prohibit it.

Core Idea
A divorced mother cannot take the child from the father's city unless to her own hometown where the marriage was contracted.

This passage addresses a common custody dispute: can a divorced mother relocate with her child away from the father's city? The Hanafi school balances the mother's right of custody (hadanah) with the father's right to maintain a relationship with his child. Drawing on a prophetic hadith and principles of custom, the ruling limits the mother's mobility to prevent harm to the father and to safeguard the child's welfare.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and default ruling
Determine what action is being judged: the divorced mother taking her child out of the city (misr). State the default hukm: she does not have that right (laysa laha dhalik).
Default is prohibition due to harm to the father.
2
Locate the exception and its conditions
The text mentions an exception: she may take the child to her own hometown (watan) but only if the husband married her there. Identify these two conditions (watan and existence of marriage in that place).
The hadith: 'Whoever marries in a city, he is one of them' supports this.
3
Analyse the dalil (hadith)
Understand the prophetic statement: by marrying in a city, the husband is deemed to have committed to residing there, so relocating the child to that city does not harm him. This is the wajh al-dalalah.
The hadith is also used to make a harbi a dhimmi by marriage.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Two narrations differ: Kitab al-Talaq denies permission to go to another city even with marriage there; al-Jami' al-Saghir allows it. The commentator prefers the denial based on custom.
The preferred view (mufta bihi) is that both conditions (watan and marriage) must coincide.
5
Apply the proximity exception
The ruling applies only when cities are distant. If close enough for the father to visit and return home, there is no harm. Similarly, moving from village to city is allowed for the child's benefit.
Moving from city to countryside is prohibited due to harm to the child's upbringing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

فصل واذا ارادت المطلقة ان تخرج بولدها من المصر فليس لها ذالك لما فيه من الاضرار بالاب الا ان تخرج به الى وطنها وقد كان الزوج تزوجها فيه لانه التزم المقام فيه عرفا و شر عاقال عليه السلام من تاهل ببلدة فهو منهم ولهذ ايصير الحربی به ذمیاوان ارادت الخروج الى مصر غير وطنها وقد كان التزوج فيه اشار فى الكتاب الى انه ليس لها ذالك وهذه رواية كتاب الطلاق وذكر فى الجامع الصغيران لها ذالك لان العقدمتى وجدفي مكان يوجب احكامه فيه كما يوجب البيع التسليم فى مكانه ومن جملة ذالك حق امساك الاولادوجه الاول ان التزوج فى دار الغربة ليس التزاما للمكث فيه عرفا وهذا اصح والحاصل انه لا بد من الأمرين جميعا الوطن ووجود النكاج وهذا كله اذا كان بين المصرين تفاوت اما اذا تقاربا بحيث يمكن للوالدان يطالع ولده ويبيت في بيته فلا باس به وكذالجواب فى القريتين ولو انتقلت من قرية المصر الى المصر لا بأس به لان فيه نظر الصغير حيث يتخلق بأخلاق اهل المصر وليس فيه ضرر بالاب وفي عكسه ضرر بالصغير لتخلقه باخلاق اهل السواد فليس لها ذالك

Whether the divorced woman can take her child out of the city or not. Section: When the divorced woman wants to take her child out of the city, she does not have that right, because it causes harm to the father. Unless she takes him to her own hometown, and provided that the husband had married her in that place, because he is considered to have committed to residing there customarily and legally. The Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Whoever marries in a city, he is one of them.' And for this reason, a harbi becomes a dhimmi by marrying. If she wants to go out to a city other than her hometown, and the marriage was contracted there, the author indicated in the book that she does not have that right. This is the narration of the Book of Talaq. And it is mentioned in al-Jami' al-Saghir that she does have that right, because the contract, when it occurs in a place, it obligates its rulings in that place, just as a sale requires delivery in its place, and among that is the right to keep the children. The first view is that marriage in a foreign land is not a commitment to stay there customarily, and this is more correct. The conclusion is that both matters are necessary: (her) hometown and the existence of the marriage (there). And this is all when there is distance between the two cities. But if they are close such that the father can see his child and return home to sleep, then there is no harm. Similarly, the same ruling applies to two villages. If she moves from a village of the city to the city, there is no harm, because it is in the interest of the child to acquire the morals of the city dwellers, and there is no harm to the father. In the opposite case, there is harm to the child by acquiring the morals of rural people, so she does not have that right. Translation: If the divorced woman wants to take her child outside the city, she does not have this right because it causes harm to the father. Unless she takes him to her own hometown, and provided that the husband had married her in that place, because he is considered to have committed to residing there customarily and legally. The Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Whoever marries in a city, he is one of them.' And for this reason, a harbi becomes a dhimmi by marrying. And if she wants to take the child to a city other than her hometown, and the marriage was contracted there, ...
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi ruling on whether a divorced mother may remove her child from the city of the father's residence. The default ruling is that she cannot take the child out of the city (misr) because it would harm the father's right to visitation and companionship with his child. However, there is an exception: if she takes the child to her own hometown (watan), and provided that the husband married her in that hometown, then she may do so. The reasoning is that by marrying her in her hometown, the husband is deemed to have accepted residence there customarily and legally. A hadith supports this: 'Whoever marries in a city, he is one of them.' This also extends to a harbi (enemy alien) who becomes a dhimmi (protected citizen) by marrying there. There is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: in the riwayah of Kitab al-Talaq, the mother is not allowed to go to another city even if marriage was there, but in al-Jami' al-Saghir, it is allowed because the marriage contract imposes its implications in that place. The commentator favors the first view (not allowed) based on custom. The key condition is that both the mother's hometown and the marriage location must be the same. If the cities are close (e.g., father can visit and return home), then there is no harm. Similarly, moving from a rural area to the city is allowed for the child's benefit. Moving from the city to the countryside is forbidden due to harm to the child's upbringing.

Key Terms
مطلقہ (muṭallaqah): A woman who has been divorced (by talaq).
وطن (waṭan): Hometown or place of origin where a person is considered to belong.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When is a divorced mother allowed to take her child out of the city according to the preferred Hanafi view?
A) Anytime she wishes
B) Only to her hometown where the marriage was contracted
C) To any city if the father agrees later
D) Only if the child is over seven years old

Key Takeaway
A divorced mother's right to relocate with her child is conditional on the child's welfare and the father's access, and is generally restricted to her hometown where the marriage took place.

Talaq — Divorce

Divorced Mother Moving Child Out of the City
Ruling on divorced mother moving child out of city

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling and its conditions regarding a divorced mother moving her child out of the father's city, and explain the intra-Hanafi difference based on the hadith and qiyas.

Core Idea
A divorced mother cannot move her child away unless returning to her hometown where marriage occurred, and cities are far apart.

This passage from al-Hidayah addresses a common post-divorce conflict: can the mother relocate the child to a different city? The Hanafi ruling seeks to balance the father's visitation rights and the child's welfare against the mother's freedom. The default is prohibition due to harm to the father, but exceptions exist. Understanding this ruling requires analyzing the hadith proof, the qiyas to sale, and the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf between two authoritative narrations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default ruling and its basis
Start by locating the default statement: the divorced mother does not have the right to take her child out of the city. The reason given is harm to the father. Note that this is a makruh tahrimi ruling.
The default protects the father's right of visitation and involvement.
2
Identify the exceptions and their conditions
Find the exception: she may take the child to her hometown only if the husband married her there. Both conditions (hometown and marriage location) must coincide. Also note the distance condition: if cities are close enough for daily visits, there is no prohibition.
The exception also covers moving from a village to the city, which benefits the child.
3
Understand the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Contrast the two narrations: Kitab al-Talaq (al-Mabsut) says she cannot take the child to a non-hometown city even if marriage occurred there; al-Jami' al-Saghir says she can, analogizing to sale. Determine which is mufta bihi and why.
The preferred view rejects the qiyas to sale because marriage abroad does not imply permanent residence customarily.
4
Analyze the dalil and qiyas
Examine the hadith 'من تأهل ببلدة فهو منهم' and its application. Then study the qiyas to sale: both contracts bind to the place of contract. Understand the objection that sale is different because it requires immediate delivery.
The hadith is zanni in both transmission and meaning, but supports the default ruling.
5
Apply the ruling to various scenarios
Test different cases: mother wants to go to her hometown (not where marriage occurred), to a city where marriage occurred but not her hometown, to a nearby city, from village to city. Determine each ruling and articulate the reasoning.
The key is to check both conditions and the distance factor.

Section: When the divorced woman wants to take her child out of the city, she does not have that right, because it involves harm to the father, unless she takes him to her hometown and the husband had married her there, because he has committed to residing there customarily and legally. The Prophet (peace be upon him) said: He who marries in a city becomes one of its inhabitants. For this reason, the harbi becomes a dhimmi by this. And if she wants to take him out to a city other than her hometown, even though the marriage occurred there, the book indicates she does not have that right. This is the narration of Kitab al-Talaq (in al-Mabsut). And it is mentioned in al-Jami' al-Saghir that she has that right, because wherever the contract takes place, its rulings become binding in that place, just as sale requires delivery in the place of contract. Among those rulings is the right to keep the children. The first view is that marriage in a foreign land does not imply an undertaking to stay there customarily; this is more correct. The conclusion is that both conditions are required: the hometown and the occurrence of marriage there. All this applies when there is a significant distance between the two cities. But if they are close enough that the father can visit his child and return to sleep in his own house, then there is no problem. Similarly, the answer for two villages. And if she moves from a village attached to the city to the city itself, there is no harm, because it benefits the child by adopting the manners of city dwellers and there is no harm to the father. In the opposite case, there is harm to the child by adopting the manners of villagers, so she does not have that right. Translation: If the divorced woman wishes to take her child out of the city, she does not have this right, because it involves harming the father. However, she may take him to her hometown if the husband married her there, because he has undertaken to reside there customarily and legally. The Prophet (peace be upon him) said: He who marries in a city becomes one of its inhabitants. For this reason, the harbi becomes a dhimmi by this marriage. And if she wants to take the child to a city other than her hometown, even though the marriage occurred there, the book indicates she does not have that right; this is the narration of Kitab al-Talaq (in al-Mabsut). The conclusion is that when a man marries a woman in her hometown, he also becomes a resident of that city, and that city becomes his hometown as well. For this reason, if a harbi man comes to Dar al-Islam and marries a dhimmi woman, he is considered a dhimmi. The author of al-Nihaya said that the author's statement 'for this reason, the harbi becomes a dhimmi by this' is a scribal error, because in other books of Hidayah it is stated that a musta'min if marries a dhimmi woman does not become a dhimmi, as he can divorce her and return. The author of al-Nihaya further supports that his teacher said that in the author's own copy this phrase is not present. And in Ghayat al-Bayan, it is quoted from Hafiz al-Din al-Kabir that upon comparison with the author's copy, this phrase was not found. So it is a scribal error. Some scholars wrote 'harbah' instead of 'harbi', meaning a harbiyya woman comes to Dar al-Islam and marries a dhimmi, then she becomes a dhimmiyya because her husband prevents her from going to Dar al-Harb. And Allah knows best. (Ayni Sharh al-Hidayah) And if the woman wants to take the child to a city other than her hometown, even though the marriage occurred there, the book (al-Quduri) indicates she does not have that right; this is the narration of Kitab al-Talaq. And in al-Jami' al-Saghir, it is mentioned that she has that right, because wherever the contract takes place, its rulings become binding in that place, just as sale requires delivery in the place of contract. Among the rulings of the marriage contract is the right to keep the children. The first view (Kitab al-Talaq) is that marriage in a foreign land does not customarily imply undertaking to stay, and this narration is more correct. The conclusion is that the woman will only be permitted to take the child away if two conditions are present: (1) it is her hometown, and (2) the marriage occurred there. And this detail applies when there is such distance between the two cities that the father cannot visit his child and return to sleep in his own house. If the two cities are so close that the father can visit his child whenever he wishes and return to sleep in his house, then there is no harm in the woman taking the child there. The same ruling applies to two villages. And if the woman moves from a village of the city to the city itself, there is no harm, because it benefits the child by adopting the manners of city dwellers and there is no harm to the father.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage states the Hanafi ruling on a divorced mother's right to move her child out of the city where the father resides. The default ruling is prohibition due to harm to the father, but with exceptions. The author first presents the original matn from al-Hidayah, then the Urdu commentary. The key point: the mother cannot take the child away from the city unless she goes to her own hometown and the husband married her there, because the husband is deemed to have accepted residence in that city customarily. The text mentions a hadith: 'Whoever marries in a city becomes one of its inhabitants.' The example is given of a harbi who becomes a dhimmi by marrying a dhimmi woman. However, a long note follows from al-Nihaya and other sources correcting a phrase in the matn: the statement that a harbi becomes a dhimmi by this is a scribal error; the correct view is that a musta'min does not become a dhimmi by marrying a dhimmi, as he can divorce and return. This correction is important for manuscript accuracy. The commentary then resolves the apparent dispute between two riwayahs: one from Kitab al-Talaq (in al-Mabsut) saying she cannot take the child to a non-hometown city even if marriage occurred there, and another from al-Jami' al-Saghir saying she can, based on qiyas with sale (where obligations attach to the place of contract). The preferred view (mufta bihi) is the first, because marriage in a foreign land does not imply a commitment to stay. So two conditions are needed: the destination must be her hometown AND the marriage must have occurred there. Additionally, there is a condition of distance: if cities are close so the father can visit daily, there is no harm. Also, moving from a village to the city is beneficial and allowed.

Key Terms
مطلقہ (muṭalliqa): A woman who has been divorced by her husband (revocably or irrevocably).
وطن (waṭan): The hometown or native place of the woman.
حربی (ḥarbī): A non-Muslim who is not under the protection of the Islamic state and is from a hostile territory (Dar al-Harb).
ذمی (dhimmī): A non-Muslim permanently residing in Dar al-Islam under the protection of the Islamic state, paying jizyah.

A divorced mother may only remove her child from the father's city if she returns to her hometown where the marriage occurred, and the cities are far apart.

Talaq — Divorce

Mother's Right to Move with Child After 'Iddah: Four Scenarios
Ruling on mother taking child from husband's city after 'iddah with four scenarios

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two conditions that must coincide for a divorced mother to relocate with her child after 'iddah according to the Hanafi school, and explain the proof (dalil) for this ruling.

Core Idea
A mother may only take her child to her hometown if the marriage contract was executed there.

After the 'iddah period, a divorced mother often wishes to return to her hometown. But does she have the right to take her child away from the father's city? The Hanafi school gives a conditional answer, balancing the mother's ties to her hometown with the father's right to maintain proximity to his child. In this lesson, we explore the four scenarios derived by the scholars of Al-Inayah and the proof from hadith and custom.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
The act is the mother taking her child out of the husband's city after the 'iddah. The default ruling is that she does not have this right, because it would harm the father.
2
Recognize the exception and its conditions
The exception is when the mother wants to take the child to her own hometown (watan) AND the marriage contract was executed in that same town. Both conditions must coincide.
This gives four possible scenarios depending on the combination of the two factors.
3
Examine the proof (dalil)
The proof is twofold: custom ('urf) and a hadith. Customarily, a husband resides in the city where he marries. The hadith (from 'Uthman) states that a man who marries in a city becomes part of that city for residency rulings.
The hadith is narrated by Ibn Abi Shaybah in his Musannaf.
4
Apply the proof to derive the condition
Since the husband is considered a resident of the marriage city, he has a right to be near his child. The mother cannot move the child away from that city unless she returns to her own hometown where the marriage was also contracted, because then the husband has already accepted that location as his residence.
5
Consider the benefit for the child
The text also mentions benefit for the child: staying in a city helps him learn civilised manners, whereas moving to a village may harm his upbringing. This factor supports the restriction.
This is a secondary consideration strengthening the ruling.

تشریح مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ عدت پوری ہونے کے بعد اگر مطلقہ عورت نے چاہا کہ اپنے بچہ کو اس شہر سے باہر لے جائے تو اسکو یہ اختیار نہیں ہے۔ کیونکہ ایسا کرنے میں باپ کو اپنے بچہ کی جدائی سے صدمہ ہوگا ۔ ہاں اگر عورت اس بچہ کو اپنے وطن لے جانا چاہے۔ درانحالیکہ شوہر نے اس عورت سے اس مقام میں نکاح کیا تھا۔ تو عورت کو اس کا اختیار ہے۔ صاحب عنایہ نے اس مسئلہ کی چار صورتیں ذکر کی ہیں ۔ اسلئے کہ عورت بچہ کو یا تو اپنے وطن لیکر جائے گی ، اور حال یہ کہ عقد نکاح بھی وہیں واقع ہوا ہے۔ یا اپنے وطن کے علاوہ لے کر جائے گی ۔ حالانکہ وہاں عقد نکاح بھی نہیں واقع ہوا ۔ یا عورت بچہ کو اپنے وطن لے جائے گی حالانکہ وہاں عقد واقع نہیں ہوا ہے۔ اور یا اپنے وطن کے علاوہ لے جائے گی ۔ اور وہیں عقد واقع ہوا ہے۔ پس اگر دو باتیں جمع ہو جائیں وطن اور وجود عقد نکاح ۔ تو بچہ کو وہاں لے جانا جائز ہے ورنہ نہیں۔ جواز کی صورت میں دلیل یہ ہوگی کہ عورت کے وطن میں نکاح کرنے سے عرفاً اور شرعاً شوہر نے وہیں قیام کرنا اپنے اوپر لازم کر لیا تھا۔ عرفاً تو اسلئے کہ شوہر عادہ اس شہر میں قیام کرتا ہے جس میں نکاح کرتا ہے۔ اور شرعا اس لئے کہ حضور اقدس صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم نے فرمایا: من تاهل ببلدة فهو منهم یعنی جس مرد نے کسی شہر میں نکاح کیا تو یہ بھی انھیں میں سے ہے۔ اور ابن ابی شیبہ نے اپنے مصنف میں حضرت عثمان سے روایت کی کہ انھوں نے منیٰ میں پوری چار رکعت پڑھیں۔ پھر فرمایا: قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم من تاهل في بلدة فهو من اهلها يصلى بصلاة المقيم وانى تاهلت مند قدمت مكة - یعنی رسول اللہ صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم نے فرمایا ہے کہ جس مرد نے کسی شہر میں نکاح کر لیا تو وہ اس شہر والوں میں سے ہو جاتا ہے کہ مقیم کی نماز پڑھے۔ اور جس وقت مکہ میں داخل ہوا تو میں نے ایک عورت سے نکاح کر لیا۔ لہذا عورت کو ایسا کرنے کا اختیار نہیں۔ کیونکہ اس میں بچہ کے حق میں بہتری ہے کہ وہ شہر والوں کے اخلاق سیکھ جائے گا۔ اور اس میں باپ کے حق میں بھی کچھ ضرر نہیں۔ اور اگر اس کے برعکس اس نے شہر سے نکل کر گاؤں میں لے جانا چاہا تو بچہ کے حق میں ضرر ہے۔ کیونکہ گنواروں کے اخلاق سیکھے گا۔
Explanation of the issue: After the completion of the 'iddah, if the divorced woman wishes to take her child out of this city, she does not have this option. Because in doing so, the father would be hurt by the separation from his child. Yes, if the woman wants to take the child to her hometown, while the husband had married her in that place, then she has that option. The author of Al-Inayah mentioned four scenarios for this issue: (1) The woman takes the child to her hometown and the marriage contract took place there. (2) She takes the child elsewhere, and the marriage contract did not take place there. (3) She takes the child to her hometown but the marriage did not take place there. (4) She takes the child elsewhere, and the marriage contract took place there. So if two things coincide — hometown and the existence of the marriage contract — then taking the child there is permissible; otherwise not. In the case of permissibility, the proof is that by marrying in the woman's hometown, the husband, customarily and legally, obliged himself to reside there. Customarily because the husband usually resides in the city where he marries. Legally because the Holy Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Whoever marries in a city, he is considered one of them.' And Ibn Abi Shaybah narrated in his Musannaf from 'Uthman that he prayed four rak'ahs in Mina, then said: 'The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: Whoever marries in a city, he is among its people; he prays the prayer of a resident. And when I entered Mecca, I married a woman.' Therefore, the woman does not have the option to do so, because in this there is benefit for the child, as he will learn the manners of the city dwellers, and there is no harm to the father. And if, contrary to this, she wants to take him out of the city to a village, there is harm for the child because he will learn the manners of rustics.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf Ul Hadaya is an expansion (sharh) on the Hidayah's discussion of custody (hadanah) after divorce. The passage type is a ruling with proof, discussing the mother's right to relocate with her child after the 'iddah period. The author, following Al-Inayah, presents four scenarios based on two factors: the mother's hometown and the location of the marriage contract. The ruling is that the mother may only take the child to her hometown if the marriage was contracted there; otherwise she cannot. The proof comes from both custom ('urf) and a hadith of the Prophet (peace be upon him) narrated by 'Uthman, which states that a man who marries in a city becomes part of that city for residency rulings. The rationale is to protect the father's right to be near his child and to ensure the child's upbringing in a civilized environment. Note that these are Hanafi rulings based on the principles of istihsan and maslahah, giving priority to the father's connection to the child while considering the mother's ties to her hometown. The four scenarios systematically cover all possibilities, showing the jurist's precision.

Key Terms
وطن (watan): The mother's hometown; the place of origin which she may return to after divorce.
عقد نکاح (aqd al-nikah): The marriage contract; its location is a factor in determining where the husband is deemed to reside.
عدت (iddah): The waiting period after divorce before a woman may remarry; its completion triggers the custody discussion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In which scenario can a divorced mother take her child to her hometown?
A) When she takes the child to her hometown regardless of where the marriage occurred.
B) When she takes the child to her hometown and the marriage contract was also in that town.
C) When she takes the child to a different town than her hometown where the marriage occurred.
D) She can never take the child out of the husband's city.

Key Takeaway
A divorced mother may only relocate with her child to her own hometown if the marriage contract was executed in that same place; otherwise, she must not cause harm to the father's right to be near his child.

Maintenance and Housing for the Divorced Woman: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
States the Hanafi ruling and refutes the Shafi'i position using proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on nafaqah and suknah for a divorced woman, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using textual and rational evidence.

Core Idea
Maintenance for a divorced woman is wajib in all cases, because she is retained for lineage.

This passage delves into a classical dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding whether a divorced woman deserves financial support and housing during her waiting period ('iddah). While both schools agree for revocable divorces, the disagreement centers on irrevocably divorced women. The Hanafi school, in a bold move, extends maintenance to all divorced women, using a unique principle of 'retention' (ihtibas) and a bold critique of a famous hadith. Prepare to understand the reasoning behind this lenient view.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify what the Hanafi school rules regarding nafaqah and suknah for a divorced woman, noting the distinction between raj'i and ba'in divorce.
The text explicitly says both are entitled.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Who disagrees and what is their position? Imam al-Shafi'i holds that only raj'i and pregnant women receive maintenance.
The Shafi'i opinion is quoted directly.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Examine the evidence the Shafi'i school presents: the hadith of Fatimah bint Qays and the analogy to a widow.
These are the main pillars of their argument.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
How does the Hanafi school refute the Shafi'i proofs? They challenge the hadith's authenticity and reinterpret the Quran.
Pay attention to the role of Umar's rejection.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
What is the practical difference between the two schools? How does it affect a woman's life?
This clarifies why the dispute matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في طلاق الرجل امرأته فلها النفقة والسكنى في عدتها رجعيا كان أو بائنا. وقال الشافعي: لا نفقة للبائنة إلا أن تكون حاملا. أما الرجعي فلان النكاح بعده قائم لاسيما عندنا فإنه يحل له الوطء. وأما البائن فاحتجوا بقول فاطمة بنت قيس قالت: طلقني زوجي ثلاثا فلم يفرض لي رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم سكنى ولا نفقة. ولأنه ملك له وهي مرتبة على الملك، ولهذا لا تجب للمتوفى عنها زوجها لانعدامه، بخلاف ما إذا كانت حاملا لانعقاد الولد، وهو قوله تعالى: {وإن كن أولات حمل فأنفقوا عليهن} الآية. ولنا أن النفقة جزاء احتباس على ما ذكرنا، والاحتباس قائم في حق حكم مقصود بالنكاح وهو الولد إذ العدة واجبة لصيانة الولد فتجـب النفقة، ولهذا كان لها السكنى بالاجماع، وصار كما إذا كانت حاملا. وحديث فاطمة بنت قيس رده عمر فقال: لا ندع كتاب ربنا وسنة نبينا بقول امرأة لا ندري صدقت أم كذبت، حفظت أم نسيت. سمعت رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم يقول: للمطلقة ثلاثا النفقة والسكنى ما دامت في العدة. ورده أيضا زيد بن ثابت وأسامة بن زيد وجابر وعائشة.
Section: When a man divorces his wife, she has the right to maintenance and housing during her 'iddah, whether the divorce is revocable or irrevocable. Al-Shafi'i said: There is no maintenance for the irrevocably divorced woman unless she is pregnant. As for the revocable, the marriage still exists, especially in our school, for he is permitted to have intercourse. As for the irrevocable, they adduced the statement of Fatimah bint Qays who said: 'My husband divorced me three times, and the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) did not prescribe for me housing nor maintenance.' And because he owns her (in a sense) and maintenance is based on ownership, hence it is not obligatory for a woman whose husband has died due to the absence of ownership, contrary to the case when she is pregnant because the child is held back, and it is His saying: {And if they are pregnant, then spend on them}. And for us, maintenance is compensation for retention (ihtibas) as we have mentioned, and retention exists with regard to the purpose of marriage, which is offspring, for the 'iddah is obligatory for the preservation of offspring, so maintenance becomes obligatory, and for this reason she has the right to housing by consensus, and it is like the case when she is pregnant. And the hadith of Fatimah bint Qays was rejected by 'Umar, who said: 'We do not abandon the Book of our Lord and the Sunnah of our Prophet for the statement of a woman; we do not know whether she spoke truthfully or lied, whether she remembered or forgot.' And he heard the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) say: 'For the woman divorced three times, maintenance and housing as long as she is in her 'iddah.' And it was also rejected by Zaid bin Thabit, Usamah bin Zaid, Jabir, and 'A'ishah.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute concerning the entitlement of a divorced woman to maintenance (nafaqah) and housing (suknah) during her 'iddah (waiting period). The Hanafi position is clear: every divorced woman, whether by revocable (raj'i) or irrevocable (ba'in) divorce, is entitled to both maintenance and housing during her 'iddah. Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees, holding that only a revocably divorced woman and a pregnant woman receive maintenance; an irrevocably divorced woman (unless pregnant) receives nothing. The Shafi'i proof includes the hadith of Fatimah bint Qays who reported that the Prophet ﷺ did not give her housing or maintenance after her threefold divorce. The Hanafi response is twofold. First, they argue that maintenance is compensation for 'retention' (ihtibas) — the woman is retained in the marriage home for the sake of preserving the lineage (offspring), and this retention exists in all divorces because 'iddah serves to protect lineage. Therefore, maintenance is due. Second, they reject the hadith of Fatimah bint Qays, citing that prominent Companions like 'Umar, Zaid bin Thabit, Usamah bin Zaid, Jabir, and 'A'ishah rejected it. 'Umar famously said: 'We do not abandon the Book of our Lord and the Sunnah of our Prophet for the statement of a woman.' They also preserve a version where the Prophet ﷺ explicitly grants maintenance to the thrice-divorced woman. Thus, the Hanafi school relies on the Quranic verse {وإن كن أولات حمل فأنفقوا عليهن} (65:6) which implies maintenance for pregnant women, and by analogy, all divorced women are entitled, as the 'iddah itself is for the protection of lineage.

Key Terms
نفقہ (nafaqah): Maintenance, including food, clothing, and lodging, that a husband must provide for his divorced wife during 'iddah.
سكنى (suknah): Housing provided to the divorced woman during her 'iddah period.
عدت (iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death of her husband before remarriage.
احتباس (ihtibas): Retention of the woman in the marital home during 'iddah for the sake of preserving lineage.
بائن (ba'in): An irrevocable divorce that ends the marriage completely; the husband cannot take her back without a new marriage.
رجعی (raj'i): A revocable divorce where the husband can take his wife back during the 'iddah without a new contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in the passage, what is the basis for obligating maintenance for a divorced woman during her 'iddah?
A) The hadith of Fatimah bint Qays
B) The principle of ihtibas (retention) for the sake of offspring
C) The analogy to a widow
D) The husband's ownership of the wife

Key Takeaway
A divorced woman is entitled to maintenance and housing in all cases during her 'iddah in the Hanafi school, based on the principle of ihtibas (retention) and the Quranic verse on pregnant women.
