Nafaqa for the Divorcée During Iddah: Proof and Rebuttal
Proof of nafaqa for bā'inah divorcée with rebuttal of Fatima hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi evidence for nafaqa during iddah and explain the rebuttal of the Fatima bint Qais hadith.

Core Idea
Nafaqa during iddah is compensation for the wife's confinement (ihtibās), making it wajib.

The Hanafi school uniquely affirms nafaqa and housing for a triply divorced woman during iddah, based on the principle of ihtibās (confinement). This lesson examines the proof from the hadith of Umar and the rebuttal of the opposing hadith of Fatima bint Qais.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core legal claim
The passage asserts that nafaqa is compensation for ihtibās, which exists during iddah. Identify the key terms: ihtibās (confinement), 'iwad (compensation).
2
Examine the analogy (qiyas)
Confinement during iddah is analogous to confinement during marriage. Since nafaqa is wajib during marriage due to ihtibās, it remains wajib during iddah.
Note the 'illah: confinement for the husband's right.
3
Analyze the opposing evidence
The hadith of Fatima bint Qais claims the Prophet did not give her nafaqa or suknā. Identify who rejected this hadith: Umar, Zayd ibn Thabit, Usamah, Jabir, Aisha.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi rebuttal
The opposing hadith is weak because it contradicts the Quranic verse (65:6) and a stronger marfu' hadith from Umar. The Hanafi position is upheld by the consensus of major Companions.
Umar's statement: 'We will not abandon the Book of our Lord and the Sunnah of our Prophet for the saying of a woman.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب النفقة

اور ہمارى دلیل یہ ہے کہ نفقہ عورت کو اپنے پاس روک رکھنے کا عوض ہے۔ جیسا کہ ہم نے اول باب الفقہ میں ذکر کیا ہے اور یہ احتباس (روکنا) مقصود نکاح یعنی بچہ کے حق میں ابھی ابھی موجود ہے کیونکہ عدت اسی واسطے واجب ہوتی ہے کہ بچہ کی حفاظت کی جائے۔ پس جب احتباس موجود ہے تو عورت کے واسطے نفقہ بھی واجب ہوگا اور اسی احتباس کی وجہ سے مطلقہ بائنہ کیلئے بالاجماع سکنیٰ واجب ہے اور یہ ایسی صورت ہو گئی جیسے کہ وہ عورت حاملہ ہو۔

اور فاطمہ بنت قیس کی حدیث کا جواب یہ ہے کہ اس حدیث کو حضرت عمر نے رد کر دیا۔ چنانچہ کہا کہ ہم اپنے پروردگار کی کتاب اور اپنے پیغمبر صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم کی سنت ایک عورت کے کہنے سے نہیں چھوڑیں گے۔ ہم نہیں جانتے کہ وہ کچی ہے یا جھوٹی ہے اور اس کو یاد رکھنا بھول گئی۔ میں نے رسول اللہ صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم سے سنا آپ صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم فرماتے تھے کہ جس عورت کو تین طلاقیں دی جائیں اس کیلئے نفقہ اور سکنیٰ واجب ہے جب تک وہ عدت میں رہے۔ اور حدیث فاطمہ بنت قیس کو زید بن ثابت، اسامہ بن زید، جابر بن عبداللہ اور ام المومنین عائشہ رضی اللہ تعالیٰ عنہم نے رد کر دیا۔ حضرت عمرؓ کے قول کتاب ربنا سے مراد آیت {أَسْكِنُوهُنَّ مِنْ حَيْثُ سَكَنْتُمْ مِنْ وُجْدِكُمْ} اور سنت مینا سے مراد حضرت عمر کا قول سمعت رسول الله يقول للمطلقة الحدیث ہے۔
Chapter of Nafaqa. And our evidence is that nafaqa is compensation for the woman confining herself (ihtibās). As we mentioned in the first chapter of fiqh, and this confinement (restraining) is present in the purpose of marriage, i.e., for the child, because iddah is only obligatory to protect the child. So when confinement exists, nafaqa also becomes obligatory for the woman, and due to this confinement, suknā (housing) is obligatory by consensus for the bā'inah divorcée, and this becomes like the case where the woman is pregnant. And the answer to the hadith of Fatima bint Qais is that Umar rejected this hadith. Thus he said: We will not abandon the Book of our Lord and the Sunnah of our Prophet (peace be upon him) for the saying of a woman; we do not know whether she remembers correctly or lies, and she may have forgotten. I heard the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) say: For a woman who is given three divorces, nafaqa and suknā are obligatory as long as she is in iddah. And the hadith of Fatima bint Qais was rejected by Zaid bin Thabit, Usamah bin Zaid, Jabir bin Abdullah, and Umm al-Mu'minin Aisha (may Allah be pleased with them). Umar's saying 'the Book of our Lord' refers to the verse {Lodge them where you lodge according to your means}, and 'the Sunnah of our Prophet' refers to Umar's statement: I heard the Messenger of Allah say about the divorcée... the hadith.
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof. It establishes that a divorcée (especially one with three irrevocable divorces) is entitled to maintenance (nafaqa) and housing (sukna) during her iddah period. The Hanafi evidence is that nafaqa is compensation ('iwad) for the wife's confinement (ihtibās) — she restrains herself for the husband's right. Since the purpose of iddah is to protect lineage (i.e., to ascertain paternity), this confinement is present. Therefore nafaqa is due, just as housing is due by consensus. The passage then rebuts the counter-evidence from the hadith of Fatima bint Qais, which claimed that a triply divorced woman gets nothing. The rebuttal is that this hadith was rejected by many senior Companions including Umar, and they relied on the general Quranic command and a stronger prophetic tradition. Thus the Hanafi position stands.

Key Terms
احتباس (ihtibās): The wife's confinement/restraining of herself for the husband's right, considered the basis for nafaqa.
مطلقہ بائنہ (mughallaqah / bā'inah): A woman who has been given three divorces, resulting in irrevocable separation (baynūnah kubrā).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi evidence in this passage, what is the primary reason nafaqa is wajib for a divorcée during iddah?
A) Because she performed household work during marriage
B) Because she is confined (ihtibās) for the husband's right, similar to the marriage period
C) Because the husband forced her to leave
D) Because she has custody of children

Key Takeaway
Nafaqa and suknā for the triply divorcée are wajib in Hanafi fiqh because ihtibās continues during iddah.

No Nafaqa for the Widow During Iddah
Ruling with two proofs and mention of differing opinions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on nafaqa for a widow during iddah and explain the two proofs that support it.

Core Idea
A widow's iddah is an act of worship, not compensation for confinement, so no nafaqa is due.

This passage addresses a common but often misunderstood ruling: a widow (mutawaffā 'anhā) is not entitled to maintenance (nafaqa) during her iddah. The text provides two rational proofs and notes the views of other schools. Understanding this distinction is crucial because it separates the widow's iddah from that of a divorcée, which carries different financial obligations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
State the core ruling: the widow receives no nafaqa during iddah. Note that the text explicitly says 'لا نفقة للمتوفى عنها زوجها'.
This is the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school.
2
Examine the First Proof
The first proof: the widow's confinement (ihtibās) is not for the husband's right but for the right of the Sharīʿah. The iddah is an act of worship (taʿabbud) from her. This is supported by the observation that detecting pregnancy is not the primary purpose—even if no menses occurs, the iddah expires after four months and ten days.
Thus, nafaqa, which compensates for confinement for the husband's right, is not due.
3
Examine the Second Proof
The second proof: nafaqa is owed incrementally (shayʾan fa-shayʾan). After death, the husband's ownership ceases, and his property transfers to heirs. It is impossible to impose an incremental obligation on the heirs' property.
This is a rational (ʿaqlī) argument based on the nature of property and obligation.
4
Understand the Nature of Widow's Iddah
Contrast the widow's iddah with that of a divorcée. The divorcée's iddah is primarily to ascertain pregnancy and preserve the husband's right to lineage, so nafaqa is wajib. The widow's iddah is primarily a religious duty without the same purpose.
This distinction is key to the Hanafi position.
5
Compare Cross-Madhhab Views
Note that Imam Malik and some Shāfiʿīs hold that suknā (housing) is wajib for the widow. Another Shāfiʿī opinion allows spending from her share of inheritance if the deceased left wealth. The Hanafi position rejects both nafaqa and suknā.
The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilāf) is that in the Hanafi school the widow receives nothing, while other schools may provide housing or maintenance.

Source Text

متوفی عنہا زوجہا کا نفقہ لازم نہیں ہے۔

ولا نفقة للمتوفى عنها زوجها لان احتباسها ليس لحق الزوج بل لحق الشرع فان التربص عبادة منها الاترى ان معنى التعرف عن براءة الرحم ليس بمراعى فيه حتى لا يشترط فيه الحيض فلانجب نفقتها عليه ولان النفقة تجب شيئا فشيئا ولا ملك له بعد الموت فلايمكن ايجابها في ملك الورثة

ترجمہ: اور متوفی عنہا زوجہا کیلئے نفقہ نہیں ہے۔ اسلئے کہ اس کا احتباس حق زوج کی وجہ سے نہیں ہے بلکہ حق شرع کی وجہ سے ہے۔ اسلئے کہ تربص (اپنے آپ کو روکنا) متوفی عنہا زوجہا کی جانب سے عبادت ہے۔ کیا یہ نہیں دیکھتے کہ رحم کی پاکی دریافت کرنا اس عدت میں ملحوظ نہیں ہے۔ حتی کہ اس عدت میں حیض شرط ہی نہیں ہے۔ پس شوہر پر متوفی عنہا زوجہا کا نفقہ واجب نہیں ہوگا اور اسلئے کہ نفقہ تھوڑا تھوڑا کر کے واجب ہوتا ہے اور شوہر کی ملک موت کے بعد باقی نہیں رہی۔ تو ورثہ کی ملک میں نفقہ واجب کرنا ممکن نہیں ہے۔

تشریح: مسئلہ ! اگر کسی عورت کا شوہر مر گیا تو اس عورت کے واسطے عدت کا نفقہ نہیں ہے۔ اس کے قائل امام احمد ہیں اور امام شافعی کا ایک قول یہی ہے اور امام شافعی کا دوسرا قول یہ ہے کہ اگر میت نے مال کثیر چھوڑا ہے تو عورت کے حصہ میراث میں سے اس پر خرچ کیا جائے اور اگر مال کم ہے تو جمیع مال ہے اس پر خرچ کیا جائے اور متوفی عنہا زوجہا کے واسطے سکنیٰ واجب ہونے میں امام شافعی کے دوقول ہیں۔ ایک تو یہ کہ اس کیلئے سکنیٰ واجب نہیں ہے جیسا کہ احناف کہتے ہیں اور دوسرا قول یہ ہے کہ اس کے واسطے سکنیٰ واجب ہے اور اسی کے قائل امام مالک ہیں۔

متوفی عنہا زوجہا کے واسطے نفقہ واجب نہ ہونے کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ عورت کا اپنے آپ کو روکے رکھنا شوہر کے حق کی وجہ سے نہیں ہے بلکہ شریعت کے حق کی وجہ سے ہے۔ اسلئے کہ تربص (روکنا) جو قرآن میں مذکور ہے یہ متوفی عنہا زوجہا کی طرف سے عبادت ہے۔ چنانچہ رحم کی پاکی دریافت کرنا متوفی عنہا زوجہا کی عدت میں ملحوظ نہیں ہے۔ حتی کہ اس عدت میں حیض شرط ہی نہیں ہے۔ چنانچہ اگر چار ماہ دس دن تک کوئی حیض نہ آوے تو بھی عدت گزر جائے گی۔ پس اس عدت میں شوہر متوفی پر نفقہ واجب نہیں ہوگا۔

اور اسلئے کہ نفقہ تھوڑا تھوڑا کر کے واجب ہوتا ہے اور موت کے بعد شوہر کی ملک باقی نہیں رہی۔ پس ورثاء کی ملک میں نفقہ واجب کرنا ممکن نہیں ہے۔
There is no nafaqa for the woman whose husband has died. Because her confinement (ihtibās) is not for the right of the husband but for the right of the Shari'ah; indeed, the waiting (tarabbus) is an act of worship from her. Do you not see that the meaning of ascertaining the purity of the womb is not considered in this iddah to the extent that menstruation is not a condition? Therefore we do not make nafaqa obligatory upon him (the deceased husband). And because nafaqa is owed bit by bit, and he has no ownership after death, so it is not possible to obligate it in the property of the heirs. ... Commentary: The issue: If a woman's husband dies, then there is no nafaqa for that woman during iddah. Imam Ahmad holds this view, and Imam Shafi'i has one opinion that agrees. Imam Shafi'i's second opinion is that if the deceased left abundant wealth, then expenditure is made from her share of inheritance; if wealth is little, then from the entire estate. Regarding suknā being wajib for the widow, Imam Shafi'i has two opinions: one that it is not wajib, as the Hanafis say; the second that it is wajib, and Imam Malik also holds this. The evidence that nafaqa is not wajib for the widow is that the woman's restraint is not for the husband's right but for the right of the Shari'ah; because the waiting (tarabbus) mentioned in the Quran is an act of worship from the widow. Thus ascertaining the purity of the womb is not considered in her iddah to the extent that menstruation is not a condition; if no menses occurs during four months ten days, the iddah still expires. Therefore nafaqa is not wajib on the deceased husband. And because nafaqa is owed bit by bit, and after death the husband's ownership does not remain, it is not possible to obligate it in the property of the heirs.
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof. It establishes that a widow (mutawaffā 'anhā) does not receive nafaqa during her iddah. Two proofs are given: (1) The widow's confinement (ihtibās) is not for the husband's right but is an act of worship (ta'abbud) for the right of Allah. The purpose of this iddah is not to protect lineage (since the husband is dead, pregnancy detection is not the primary goal; indeed, even if no menses occurs, the iddah expires after four months ten days). Therefore, nafaqa, which is compensation for confinement for the husband's right, is not due. (2) Nafaqa accrues daily, but after death the husband's property is transferred to heirs. Obligating nafaqa from the estate would impose a new debt on the heirs without proper cause. The text then notes that Imam Ahmad and Imam Shafi'i (in one opinion) agree, while Shafi'i's second opinion allows spending from her inheritance if the deceased left wealth. For suknā (housing), Hanafis say it is not wajib; Malik holds it is wajib, and Shafi'i has two opinions.

Key Terms
تربص (tarabbus): The waiting period; for a widow, it is an act of worship (ta'abbud) without the primary purpose of detecting pregnancy.
متوفی عنہا زوجہا (mutawaffā 'anhā zawjuhā): A woman whose husband has died; she observes iddah of four months and ten days.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because she is not confined (ihtibās) at all
B) Because her confinement is not for the husband's right but is an act of worship, and nafaqa is incremental and cannot be imposed on the deceased's estate
C) Because she inherits enough from the deceased
D) Because she is expected to remarry quickly

Key Takeaway
A widow receives no nafaqa during iddah because her confinement is an act of worship, not compensation for the husband's right.

Nafaqa in Separation Due to Wife's Sin
Ruling with proof analogizing sinful separation to nāshizah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that distinguishes sinful and non-sinful causes of separation and determine the wife's entitlement to nafaqa during iddah.

Core Idea
Sinful separation forfeits nafaqa unless she remains free and in the marital home.

This passage from the Hanafi tradition clarifies a nuanced rule: a woman who causes separation through her own sinful conduct loses her right to nafaqa during the waiting period (iddah). But not all wife-initiated separations are treated equally; non-sinful causes preserve the right. The ruling rests on the distinction between confinement with right (bi haqq) and without right (bi ghayri haqq).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is separation (farqah) caused by the wife's sin (ma'siyah) such as apostasy or kissing the husband's son with desire. The ruling: no nafaqa for her during iddah.
The ruling is analogized to the case of a nāshizah (rebellious wife) who also loses nafaqa.
2
Locate the analogy
The wife is compared to a nāshizah because both confine themselves without right (bi ghayri haqq). The 'illah is the unjustified confinement.
This is a clear qiyas (analogy) from the established ruling of nāshizah.
3
Note the key condition for apostasy
For apostasy, the text from al-Mabsūt specifies: if the wife is not imprisoned but remains in the marital home, nafaqa is still wajib. Forfeiture only occurs if she is imprisoned.
Imprisonment (habs) is the operative cause for loss, not apostasy per se.
4
Contrast with non-sinful separations
When separation occurs without sin (e.g., wife exercises khiyār al-'itq, khiyār al-bulūgh, separation due to lack of kafā'ah, li'ān, khul', īlā'), nafaqa and suknā remain wajib because confinement is with right (bi haqq).
This includes cases where wife withholds herself to collect prompt mahr.

وكل فرقة جاءت من قبل المرأة بمعصية مثل الردة وتقبيل ابن الزوج فلا نفقة لها لانها صارت حابسة نفسها بغير حق فصارت كما اذا كانت ناشزة بخلاف المهر بعد الدخول لأنه وجد التسليم في حق المهر بالوطى وبخلاف ما اذا جاءت الفرقة من قبلها بغير معصية كخيار العتق وخيار البلوغ والتفريق لعدم الكفاءة لانها حبست نفسها بحق وذالك لا يسقط النفقة كما اذا حبست نفسها لا ستيفاء المهر

ترجمہ: اور ہر جدائی جو معصیت کی وجہ سے عورت کی جانب سے آئی ہو۔ مثلاً عورت مرتدہ ہو گئی یا شہوت کے ساتھ اس نے شوہر کے بیٹے کا بوسہ لے لیا۔ تو اس کے واسطے نفقہ نہ ہوگا۔ کیونکہ اس کا اپنے نفس کو روکنا ناحق ہو گیا ہے۔ تو ایسی ہوگئی جیسے وہ نافرمانی کر کے (گھر سے نکل گئی) برخلاف دخول کے بعد مہر کے اسلئے کہ مہر کے حق میں تسلیم پایا گیا ہے۔ اور برخلاف اس صورت کے جبکہ فرقت عورت کی جانب سے بغیر معصیت کے پیدا ہوئی۔ جیسے خیار عتق اور خیار بلوغ اور کفو نہ ہونے کی وجہ سے تفریق ۔ کیونکہ اس نے اپنے نفس کو حق کے ساتھ روکا ہے۔ اور یہ نفقہ ساقط نہیں کرتا ہے جیسا اگر اس نے اپنے آپ کو مہر وصول کرنے کی وجہ سے روکا ہو۔

تشریح: مسئلہ اگر فرقت معصیت کی وجہ سے عورت کی جانب سے پیدا ہو۔ مثلاً عورت اسلام سے پھر گئی یا اس نے شہوت کیساتھ اپنے شوہر کے بیٹے کا بوسہ لے لیا تو اس کے واسطے نفقہ نہ ہوگا۔ اسلئے کہ وہ اپنے نفس کو ناحق روکنے والی ہے۔ پس یہ ایسی ہوگئی جیسے وہ نافرمانی کر کے گھر سے نکل گئی ہو۔ مصنف نے عبارت میں نفقہ کا ذکر کیا ہے۔ اسلئے کہ سکنی اس معتدہ کے واسطے بھی واجب ہوگا ۔ کیونکہ معتدہ کیلئے گھر میں مہرے رہنا واجب ہے۔ لہذا عورت کی معصیت سے سکنی ساقط نہیں ہوگا۔ مبسوط میں لکھا ہے کہ مردہ کا نفقہ مین ردت کی وجہ سے ساقط نہیں ہوا۔ بلکہ اسلئے ساقط ہو گا کہ ردت کی وجہ سے وہ قید اور محبوس کر لی جائے گی اور محبوسہ بحق (جو اپنے حق میں گرفتار کر لی گئی ہو) بحالت قیام نکاح نفقہ کی مستحق نہیں ہوتی۔ پس ایسے ہی عدت میں بھی نفقہ کی مستحق نہیں ہوگی ۔ حتی کہ اگر عورت مرتدہ ہوگئی اور محبوس نہیں کی گئی بلکہ شوہر کے گھر میں ہے تو اس کے واسطے نفقہ واجب ہوگا۔ اسلئے کہ جبس اور قید کرنا نہیں پایا گیا ۔ (ہندیہ، عنایہ) اور برخلاف اس کے کہ اگر عورت وطی کے بعد متردہ ہوئی تو اس کا مہر ساقط نہیں ہوگا۔ کیونکہ مہر ملک بضع کا عوض ہوتا ہے اور عورت کی جانب سے وطی کے ساتھ تسلیم بضع پایا گیا۔ اسلئے شوہر کے ذمہ سے مہر ساقط نہیں ہوگا اور اس کے برخلاف اگر فرقت عورت کی جانب سے بغیر معصیت کے پیدا ہوئی ۔ مثلاً خیار عتق اور خیار بلوغ کی وجہ سے عورت نے تفریق کی ہے۔ یا کفو نہ ہونے کی وجہ سے تفریق کی گئی تو عدت کے زمانے میں اس عورت کے واسطے نفقہ واجب ہو گا۔ اسی طرح جس عورت نے لعان کیا اور لعان کی وجہ سے تفریق ہوئی تو اس کے واسطے بھی نفقہ اور سکنی واجب ہو گا اور اسی طرح خلع یا ایلاء کی وجہ سے بائنہ ہوگئی تو بھی اس کیلئے بھی نفقہ اور سکنی ہوگا۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ ان صورتوں میں عورت نے اپنے نفس کو حق کے ساتھ روکا ہے اور ایسا روکنا نفقہ ساقط نہیں کرتا ہے۔ جیسے اگر اس نے اپنے آپ کو شوہر کے پاس جانے سے اس واسطے روکا کہ اپنا مہر معجل وصول کرے۔
And every separation that comes from the woman's side with sin, such as apostasy and kissing the husband's son (with desire), then there is no nafaqa for her, because she has become one who confines herself without right, so she is like a nāshizah (rebellious wife). Contrary to the mahr after consummation, because there is delivery (taslīm) in the right of mahr by intercourse. And contrary to when separation comes from her side without sin, such as the option of emancipation (khiyār al-'itq), option of puberty (khiyār al-bulūgh), and separation due to lack of kafā'ah, because she confined herself with right, and that does not nullify nafaqa, just as if she confined herself to receive the mahr. Commentary: The issue: If separation occurs from the woman's side due to sin, e.g., apostasy or kissing the husband's son with desire, then no nafaqa. Because she is confining herself unjustly, so she is like a nāshizah. The author mentions only nafaqa, but suknā also is wajib for this iddah, because a woman in iddah must reside in the house; therefore sin does not nullify suknā. In al-Mabsūt it is written that the nafaqa of a female apostate is not nullified due to apostasy but because she is imprisoned; if she is not imprisoned but stays in the husband's house, nafaqa is wajib. And contrary to mahr after consummation, it is not nullified. And contrary to separation without sin, e.g., khiyār al-'itq, khiyār al-bulūgh, lack of kafā'ah, li'ān, khul', īlā' - in all these, nafaqa and suknā are wajib because she confined herself with right, just as if she confined herself to receive her prompt mahr.
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with reasoning. It deals with cases where the separation (farqah) is caused by the wife through sinful conduct (ma'siyah). Two examples are given: apostasy (riddah) and kissing the husband's son with sexual desire. In such cases, the wife is not entitled to nafaqa during iddah because her confinement (ihtibās) becomes unjust (bi ghayri haqq), making her like a nāshizah (disobedient wife). However, this does not affect mahr already due after consummation, nor does it affect suknā (residence) because the iddah residence is obligatory regardless. The text further clarifies from al-Mabsūt that if the apostate wife is not imprisoned but stays in the husband's house, she is entitled to nafaqa because imprisonment is the cause of forfeiture. Conversely, if the separation occurs without sin on the wife's side (e.g., she exercises khiyār al-'itq, khiyār al-bulūgh, separation due to lack of kafā'ah, li'ān, khul', īlā'), then nafaqa and suknā remain wajib because her confinement is with right. The principle is: ihtibās bi haqq does not nullify nafaqa; ihtibās bi ghayri haqq does.

Key Terms
فرقت (farqah): Legal separation between spouses, occurring through divorce, annulment, or dissolution.
ناشزہ (nāshizah): A wife who rebels against her husband without legal right, losing her right to nafaqa.
معصیت (ma'siyah): Sinful act that causes separation; here includes apostasy and sexual misconduct.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A woman apostatizes from Islam, and the marriage is dissolved. She is not imprisoned but continues to live in the marital house. According to the Hanafi ruling in this passage, is she entitled to nafaqa during iddah?
A) Yes, because she is not imprisoned and remains in the house
B) No, because apostasy is a major sin and forfeits all rights
C) Yes, only if the husband permits her to stay
D) No, because apostasy nullifies the marriage retroactively

Key Takeaway
Separation caused by the wife's sin forfeits nafaqa only if her confinement is unjust (e.g., imprisoned for apostasy); non-sinful separations always preserve nafaqa.

Ruling after iddah of divorced woman breastfeeding her child
States ruling on hiring a divorced woman after her iddah to breastfeed her child

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on hiring a divorced woman to breastfeed her child after her iddah and explain the proof based on the removal of marriage.

Core Idea
Once iddah expires, she is like a stranger; hiring her is permissible.

In this passage, the Hidayah clarifies a common concern: can a divorced woman be hired to breastfeed her own child? The answer depends on whether the marriage bond still exists. Once the iddah of a final divorce ends, the woman becomes a complete stranger (ajnabiyya) to her ex-husband, and hiring her is like hiring any other woman. This lesson examines the reasoning behind this rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
Determine what action is being ruled upon and what the ruling is. The act is hiring a divorced woman to breastfeed her child. The ruling is permissible (ja'iz).
The passage explicitly states 'jaza' (permissible).
2
Identify the condition that changes the ruling
The ruling depends on a key condition: the expiry of the iddah. If the iddah is still in effect, the ruling is different (not permissible). After iddah, it becomes permissible.
The text says 'wa in inqadat 'iddatuha' (and if her iddah expires).
3
Locate the proof (dalil)
The proof given is that the marriage is completely removed (nikah qad zala bilkulliyya) and she becomes like a stranger (sarat kal-ajnabiyya). This is the reasoning.
No explicit Quran or Sunnah is cited; the proof is based on the legal consequence of iddah expiry.
4
Understand the analogy (qiyas)
The ruling is derived by analogy: just as it is permissible to hire a stranger (ajnabiyya) to breastfeed, so too for the ex-wife after iddah, because the impediment (marriage) is gone.
The illa (effective cause) is the absence of marital relationship.
5
Apply the ruling to similar scenarios
Consider if the child is from a different father, or if the woman remarries. The principle remains: once the marriage is fully severed, she is like any other woman.
This ruling is specific to the mother's own child? The text says 'li-irda' waladiha' (to breastfeed her child).

And if her iddah expires, then he hires her, meaning to breastfeed her child, it is permissible because the marriage is completely removed and she becomes like a stranger. Translation: And if the iddah of the divorced woman expires, then he hires her to breastfeed his child from her, it is permissible. Because the marriage is completely gone and she becomes like a stranger. Explanation: The case is that after the divorced woman's iddah expires, the husband hires her to breastfeed his child from her; this ijarah is permissible. The proof: when the iddah expires, the marriage is completely removed and she becomes like a stranger. Just as hiring a stranger is permissible, so too is hiring her.
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage clarifies the ruling for a divorced woman after her iddah has ended. Once the iddah of an irrevocable divorce expires, all remnants of the marriage are gone. The woman is now completely a stranger (ajnabiyya) to the man. Therefore, if he wishes to hire her to breastfeed their child, it is permissible just as hiring any other woman would be. This is an exception to the earlier prohibition that applied when the marriage or iddah still existed. The reasoning is that the obligation to breastfeed for free was tied to the marriage bond; once that bond is fully severed, she is no longer obligated and may accept wages.

Key Terms
عدت (iddah): The waiting period after divorce during which the woman cannot remarry.
اجنبیہ (ajnabiyya): A woman who is not a mahram (stranger) with whom marriage is permissible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Only if she remarries

Key Takeaway
A divorced woman may be hired to breastfeed her child after her iddah ends because she becomes like a stranger.

Using Divorce Terms for Emancipation: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Examines the ruling when a master uses divorce or prohibition words intending emancipation, with Shafi'i comparison

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school rejects the use of divorce terms for emancipation even when the master intends it, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
Divorce words remove a restriction; emancipation gives power. The stronger cannot be effected by the weaker.

This passage tackles a key dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools: if a master says 'You are divorced' to his slave-girl, intending to free her, does she become free? The Hanafi school says no, emphasizing that divorce is specific to marriage and cannot be repurposed for emancipation. The Shafi'i school, prioritizing intention, says yes. Let's unpack the linguistic and legal arguments.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling given in the text: the slave-girl is not emancipated. Note that this applies to explicit divorce terms (talaq, bain) and prohibition words (tahrim) when intended for emancipation.
The ruling is clear: 'لم تعتق' (she is not emancipated).
2
Identify the opponent and their view
The text mentions Imam al-Shafi'i's position: she is emancipated if the master intended it. Understand that this is based on the principle that intention can make words metaphorical.
Opposing dalil: the two types of ownership (milk al-yamin and milk al-nikah) are similar, so words can transfer.
3
Analyze the Hanafi linguistic proof
The Hanafi rebuttal focuses on the linguistic difference: emancipation (itq) means establishing power, while divorce (talaq) means removing a restriction. The slave is likened to an inanimate object; emancipation gives life-like capacity. A married woman is already capable, just restricted by marriage.
Thus, the nature of the two acts is fundamentally different.
4
Assess the hierarchy of ownership
The text argues that milk al-yamin (ownership of a slave) is stronger than milk al-nikah (ownership in marriage). Therefore, words from the weaker category (divorce) cannot produce the stronger effect (emancipation).
This is a key argument for the Hanafi position.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: if a master utters divorce words intending emancipation, Hanafi says the slave remains in bondage; Shafi'i says she becomes free. This affects legal status, inheritance, marriage, etc.
Learn to state the practical difference concisely.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And if he said to his slave-girl, 'You are divorced' or 'you are bain' or 'you are forbidden' and intended emancipation, she is not emancipated. Al-Shafi'i said she is emancipated if he intends. And similarly on this disagreement are the rest of explicit and implicit expressions according to what their scholars said: because there is agreement between two types of ownership: each of them is ownership of the self? The apparent is ownership of the right hand, and similarly ownership of marriage is in the ruling of ownership of the self to the extent that perpetuity is a condition and specification invalidates it, and the function of both expressions is to remove what is his right, which is ownership, and for this reason suspension by condition is valid therein. As for rulings, they are established due to a prior cause, which is being a responsible person, and for this reason the words 'emancipation' and 'freeing' are suitable as metaphors for divorce, so likewise the opposite. Our response is that he intended what his word does not support, because linguistically emancipation is establishing power and divorce is removing a restriction. This is because the slave is attached to inanimate objects, and by emancipation he is enlivened and gains capability, but not so the married woman, for she is capable except that the restriction of marriage prevents, and by divorce the restriction is removed, so power appears. It is clear that the first is stronger, and because ownership of the right hand is above ownership of marriage, so...
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage addresses a scenario where a master says to his female slave (slave-girl), 'You are divorced' (taliq), 'you are bain' (irrevocably separated), or 'you are forbidden' (takhammari) with the intention of emancipating her. The Hanafi ruling is that she is not emancipated, because such words are terms for divorce, not emancipation. Imam al-Shafi'i, however, holds that if the master intends emancipation by these words, she becomes free. The passage then presents reasoning: there is a similarity between two types of ownership—ownership of property (milk al-yamin) and ownership of marriage (milk al-nikah). Both involve a right to control, but emancipation is different from divorce. Emancipation (itq) linguistically means 'establishing power' (al-quwwa) or making the slave capable, while divorce (talaq) means 'removing a restriction.' The slave is originally like an inanimate object; emancipation gives him life-like autonomy. A married woman, on the other hand, is already capable but restricted by marriage; divorce removes that restriction. The Hanafi argument concludes that the nature of emancipation is stronger and higher (because milk al-yamin is above milk al-nikah), so divorce terms cannot cause emancipation. The text is cut off but clearly maintains the Hanafi position.

Key Terms
عتق (itq): Emancipation; legally, the act of freeing a slave, which gives him full legal capacity.
طلاق (talaq): Divorce; legal dissolution of marriage, removing the restriction of marriage.
ملک الیمین (milk al-yamin): Ownership of the right hand; ownership of a slave. Considered a stronger form of ownership than marriage.

C) She is freed only if she also says 'I accept.'
D) She is considered a free person but must pay her value.

Key Takeaway
Divorce words cannot effect emancipation because they remove a restriction, while emancipation grants new legal capacity; intention alone cannot change the nature of the word.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
10 min
Divorce Words vs Emancipation: Hanafi and Shafi'i Dispute
Dispute over whether divorce words effect emancipation when intended

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that divorce words (like 'taliq') cannot effect emancipation, and contrast this with the Shafi'i view, identifying the linguistic and legal reasoning.

Core Idea
Divorce words remove a shackle; emancipation grants life — the stronger act cannot be named by the weaker term.

Imagine a master says to his slave-girl, 'You are divorced,' intending to free her. Would she become free? The Hanafi and Shafi'i schools disagree. This dispute hinges on the linguistic and legal weight of emancipation versus divorce, and the strength of different types of ownership. In this lesson, we unpack the arguments and see why the Hanafi school insists that divorce words cannot bear the meaning of emancipation.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling: the master's utterance of divorce words with intention of emancipation does NOT free the slave-girl. This is the Hanafi school's held position.
The Hanafi school is consistent across Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani.
2
State the Shafi'i position
Identify the opposing view: Imam al-Shafi'i holds that she IS freed if the master intended emancipation. The same dispute applies to all divorce words, explicit or allusive.
Imam Ahmad has two narrations, one with each school.
3
Analyze the Shafi'i proof
Understand the Shafi'i argument: both types of ownership (milk al-yamin and milk al-nikah) are ownership of the essence. Since words of emancipation ('itq, tahrir) can be used as allusions for divorce, the reverse should also be possible. The word can bear the intended meaning.
The Shafi'i rely on the symmetry of the two acts: both remove ownership.
4
Analyze the Hanafi counter-proof
Examine the Hanafi response: linguistically, i'taq means establishing strength; talaq means removing a shackle. The slave is like an inanimate object gaining life through emancipation; the married woman is already capable. Milk al-yamin is stronger than milk al-nikah, so its removal (emancipation) is also stronger. A word can only be used metaphorically for something weaker than its literal meaning, not stronger. Therefore, divorce words cannot effect emancipation.
The Hanafi reasoning is based on a qualitative hierarchy of legal acts.
5
Determine the practical consequence
The thamarah al-khilaf: if a master says 'anti taliq' intending emancipation, the Hanafi school says no emancipation; the Shafi'i says yes. This directly affects the legal status of the slave-girl.
The mufta bihi in the Hanafi school is the view that she does not become free.

ملک مین کے حکم میں ہے۔ حتی کہ نکاح کی شرط یہ ہے کہ ہمیشہ کے واسطے نیت کرے۔ جیسا کہ عقد بیع میں تابید ( ہمیشگی ) شرط ہے اور اگر نکاح وقت معین تک کیلئے ہو تو باطل ہو جاتا ہے۔ پس ثابت ہوا کہ ملک نکاح شرعاً ملک مین کے حکم میں ہے۔ و عمل اللفظين . سے ایک سوال کا جواب ہے۔ سوال یہ ہے کہ اعتاق ( آزاد کرنا ) نام ہے۔ غلام کے اند رقوت ثابت
The master said to his slave-girl: 'You are ṭāliq (divorced) or bāʾin (irrevocably divorced) or takhammarī (cover yourself with a veil)' and intended thereby emancipation – she is not freed. Imam al-Shāfiʿī's viewpoint: And if he said to his slave-girl 'You are ṭāliq or bāʾin or takhammarī' and intended emancipation, she is not freed according to us, and al-Shāfiʿī said she is freed if he intends. And likewise all clear and allusive words of divorce are subject to this dispute, as their mashāyikh said: because his word can bear that meaning. For between the two types of ownership there is congruence: each of them is ownership of the essence (milk al-ʿayn). That is apparent in milk al-yamīn (right-hand ownership), and similarly milk al-nikāḥ (marriage ownership) is in the ruling of milk al-ʿayn, such that perpetuity is a condition of it and temporal limitation invalidates it. The function of both words (in divorce and emancipation) is to remove what is his right, i.e., ownership. For this reason, suspending emancipation on a condition is valid. As for rulings, they are established by a prior cause, and that is his being morally responsible. For this reason the words 'ʿitq' and 'taḥrīr' can serve as allusions for divorce, so the opposite should also be valid. Our proof is that he intended a meaning that his word cannot bear. Because in language, iʿtāq (emancipation) means establishing strength, while ṭalāq means removing a shackle. This is because the slave is joined to inanimate objects, and by emancipation he is enlivened and gains power. The married woman is not like that, for she is capable, except that the marriage tie is an obstacle; by divorce the obstacle is removed and her power appears. There is no doubt that the first is stronger. And because milk al-yamīn is above milk al-nikāḥ, so its removal is also stronger. A word may be used metaphorically for something less than its literal meaning, not for something above it. Hence it is prevented in the disputed case, and permissible in the reverse. Translation: ... And if the master said to his slave-girl: 'You are ṭāliqah (divorced) or bāʾinah (irrevocably divorced) or wear a veil' and intended thereby emancipation, then she is not freed. Imam al-Shāfiʿī said that she is freed if the master intends. This same dispute applies to all words of divorce, whether explicit or allusive, as the Shāfiʿī mashāyikh have stated. Imam al-Shāfiʿī's proof is that the master intended a meaning that his word can bear. Because between the two types of ownership there is mutual congruence. For each of them is ownership of the essence (milk al-ʿayn). Thus in milk al-raqaba (ownership of the slave's person), ownership of the essence is apparent, and as for milk al-nikāḥ, it is also in the ruling of milk al-ʿayn, such that the condition of marriage is that it be perpetual, as in a contract of sale, and if marriage is for a fixed term, it is invalid. So it is established that marriage ownership is legally in the ruling of ownership of the essence. And 'the function of both words' is an answer to a question. The question is: Iʿtāq (emancipation) is a name. In a slave, strength is established... (text truncated at page end).
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage presents an inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafis and the Shafi'is regarding whether divorce words (such as 'taliq', 'bain', etc.) uttered to a slave-girl with the intention of emancipation actually free her. The Hanafi position is that she does not become free, while Imam al-Shafi'i holds that she does if the master intends emancipation. The text discusses the linguistic and legal reasoning: the Shafi'i argument is that both emancipation and divorce involve removing ownership, so words of divorce can metaphorically serve for emancipation. The Hanafi response distinguishes between the two: emancipation grants power to the slave (who is like an inanimate object), while divorce removes an obstacle (the marriage tie) from a free woman who already has power. Since milk al-yamin (ownership of a slave) is stronger than milk al-nikah (ownership in marriage), the word for the weaker removal (divorce) cannot be used for the stronger (emancipation). The passage also mentions that Imam Ahmad has two narrations, one siding with the Hanafis and one with the Shafi'is.

Key Terms
عتق ('itq): Emancipation of a slave; legally, the removal of ownership over a slave, granting them full legal capacity.
طلاق (talaq): Divorce; the dissolution of the marriage contract, removing the marital tie.
ملک یمین (milk al-yamin): Ownership of a slave; the legal relationship of master over a slave.
ملک نکاح (milk al-nikah): Ownership in marriage; the husband's right over his wife's sexual availability and certain aspects.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a master says to his slave-girl 'anti taliq' with the intention of freeing her, what is the ruling?
A) She becomes free because the word taliq can be used for emancipation metaphorically.
B) She does not become free because taliq is a divorce word and cannot bear the meaning of emancipation.
C) She becomes free only if the slave-girl is his wife as well.
D) She becomes free only if the master says 'anti taliq' three times.

Key Takeaway
Divorce words cannot emancipate a slave because emancipation establishes strength, while divorce only removes a shackle; the stronger act cannot be named by a weaker term.

Talaq — Divorce
advanced
12 min
Using Talaq Words for Emancipation: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Dispute on whether talaq words can effect emancipation by intention

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions on using divorce words for emancipation and explain why Hanafis reject the analogy based on the strength differential.

Core Idea
A weaker word cannot stand for a stronger act: talaq (cancellation) cannot effect itq (granting life).

This passage presents a classic inter-madhhab dispute over whether words of divorce, when uttered to a slave-girl with the intention of freeing her, actually effect emancipation. The Hanafi school says no—emancipation is a stronger legal act than divorce, so the weaker word cannot bear the stronger meaning. Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees, arguing that both involve cancelling ownership and therefore words of one can be used for the other by analogy. Let's walk through the arguments to understand the Hanafi position and its proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue and the two positions
Read the opening ruling: the master says to his slave-girl 'You are divorced' or similar words, intending emancipation. The Hanafi ruling is that she does not become free; the Shafi'i ruling is that she does. Note that this disagreement applies to all explicit and implicit divorce words.
2
Analyze the Shafi'i analogy
Understand the Shafi'i proof: both mulk yamin (ownership of slave) and mulk nikah (ownership in marriage) are considered ownership of essence (milk al-'ayn). The effect of both talaq and itq is cancellation (isqat) of that ownership. Therefore, just as words of emancipation can be used for divorce, words of divorce should be usable for emancipation.
3
Examine the Hanafi linguistic objection
The Hanafi response: in language, 'itq means granting strength (i'thbat quwwa)—the slave becomes alive and gains legal capacity. Talaq means removing the marriage tie—the wife already has capacity, only a restriction is lifted. Hence itq is stronger (aqwa). A word can be used metaphorically for something less than its literal meaning, but not for something greater. So talaq words cannot be used for itq.
4
Understand the entailment argument
Mulk yamin is stronger than mulk nikah because it entails mulk nikah (a master owns his slave-girl's marriage), but not vice versa. A stronger ownership needs a stronger cancellation. Since talaq is weaker, it cannot cancel the stronger ownership.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: in a real case, according to Hanafi the slave remains in ownership; the master can still sell her or have intercourse with her. According to Shafi'i, she becomes free immediately. Note also that the reverse—using emancipation words for divorce—is valid in both schools.

تشریح مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ اگر مولی نے اپنی باندی سے کہا انت طلاق یا انت بائن یا تخمری یعنی اوڑھنی سے اپنا منہ ڈھک اور اس سے آزاد کرنے کی نیت کی تو وہ باندی آزاد نہ ہوگی۔ امام شافعی نے فرمایا کہ اگر نیت کرے تو آزاد ہو جائے گی ۔ یہی اختلاف تمام الفاظ طلاق میں ہے۔ خواہ صریح ہوں یا کنایہ ہوں ۔ جیسا کہ مشائخ شافعیہ نے بیان کیا ہے۔ مثلاً اپنی باندی سے کہے انت مطلقة، طلقتك ثقني، اغربي انت برية، انت خلة وغيره وغيره امام احمد سے دو روایتیں ہیں۔ ایک میں احناف کے ساتھ ہیں اور ایک میں شوافع کے ساتھ ہیں۔
امام شافعی کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ مولیٰ نے اپنے کلام سے ایسے معنی مراد لیتے ہیں جن کو اس کا کلام مشتمل ہے۔ کیونکہ ملک رقبہ اور ملک نکاح کے درمیان باہم موافقت ہے اور موافقت اس طور پر ہے کہ ان دونوں میں ہر ایک ملک عین ( ذاتی ) ہے۔ چنانچہ ملک رقبہ میں ملک عین کا ہونا ظاہر و باہر ہے اور رہی ملک نکاح سو وہ بھی ملک مین کے حکم میں ہے۔ حتی کہ نکاح کی شرط یہ ہے کہ ہمیشہ کے واسطے نیت کرے۔ جیسا کہ عقد بیع میں تابید ( ہمیشگی ) شرط ہے اور اگر نکاح وقت معین تک کیلئے ہو تو باطل ہو جاتا ہے۔ پس ثابت ہوا کہ ملک نکاح شرعاً ملک مین کے حکم میں ہے۔ و عمل اللفظين . سے ایک سوال کا جواب ہے۔ سوال یہ ہے کہ اعتاق ( آزاد کرنا ) نام ہے۔ غلام کے اند رقوت ثابت کرنے کا۔ چنانچہ اعتقاق سے وہ احکام ثابت ہو جاتے ہیں جو اعتاق سے پہلے ثابت نہیں تھے ۔ مثلاً آزادی کے بعد غلام بیع اور شراء کا مالک ہو جاتا ہے۔ شہادت اور قضاء کا اہل ہو جاتا ہے اور اس کو حق ولایت حاصل ہو جاتے ہیں اور یہی معنی ہیں قوت شرعیہ کے اور طلاق اسقاط محض کا نام ہے۔ یعنی طلاق کے ذریعہ صرف شوہر کی ملک نکاح ساقط ہو جاتی ہے۔ حاصل یہ ہوا کہ اعتناق ، اثبات قوت کا نام ہے اور طلاق اسقاط محض کا۔ اس وجہ سے ان دونوں کے درمیان کوئی مناسبت نہیں ہے۔ لہذا الفاظ طلاق سے مجاز ا اور کنایہ اعتاق مراد لینا کیسے درست ہوگا۔
امام شافعی کی طرف سے جواب یہ ہے کہ دونوں لفظ یعنی لفظ طلاق اور عتاق میں سے ہر ایک کا عمل یہ ہے کہ اس کا حق ساقط کر دے۔ یعنی ملک زائل کر دے۔ پس جس طرح لفظ طلاق سے ملک نکاح زائل ہو جاتی ہے۔ اسی طرح لفظ عتاق سے ملک رقبہ زائل ہو جاتی ہے۔ حاصل یہ کہ اعتاق بھی طلاق کی طرح اسقاط ہے اور چونکہ اعتاق اسقاط ہے۔ اس لیئے اعتماق کو شرط پر معلق کرنا صحیح ہے۔ جیسا کہ طلاق کو شرط پر معلق کرنا صحیح ہے۔ پس خلاصہ یہ ہوا کہ طلاق اور اعتاق کے درمیان مناسبت موجود ہے اور جب مناسبت موجود ہے تو الفاظ طلاق کو آزاد کرنے کے معنی میں مجاز ا استعمال کیا جا سکتا ہے اور ربا احکام کا ثابت ہونا تو وہ سبب سابق کے ساتھ ہوتا ہے۔ یعنی آدمی کا مکلف عاقل بالغ آزاد ہونا۔ یعنی احکام کا ثبوت آدمیت کی وجہ سے ہوتا ہے۔ مگر رقیت ثبوت احکام کیلئے مانع تھی۔ پس اتاق کی وجہ سے جب مانع زائل ہو گیا تو اسی سبب سابق یعنی آدمیت کی وجہ سے احکام ثابت ہوں گے۔ پس ثابت ہوا کہ اعتناق اثبات قوت کا نام نہیں بلکہ طلاق کی طرح اسقاط کا نام ہے۔ پس چونکہ الفاظ طلاق اور الفاظ عتاق میں مناسبت ہے۔ اسلئے جس طرح الفاظ عتاق سے طلاق مراد لینا مجازاً درست ہے۔ اسی طرح الفاظ طلاق سے آزاد کرنے کے معنی مراد لینا بھی صحیح ہوگا۔ یعنی جس طرح کسی نے اپنی بیوی سے انت حرہ کہا اور طلاق کی نیت کی تو طلاق واقع ہو جاتی ہے۔ اسی طرح اگر کوئی شخص اپنی باندی سے انت طالق کہے اور آزاد کرنے کی نیت کرے تو وہ آزاد ہو جائے گی۔ اور ہماری دلیل یہ ہے کہ مولیٰ نے انت طالق یا بائن یا دوسرے الفاظ طلاق سے ایسے معنی مراد لیتے ہیں۔ جس معنی کا احتمال ہی لفظ نہیں رکھتے ہیں ۔ یعنی طلاق اور عتاق کے درمیان کوئی مناسبت نہیں ہے۔ کیونکہ لغت میں اعتناق کے معنی قوت دینا ہے اور طلاق کے معنی قید نکاحی کو اٹھا دینا ہے اور اعتقاق کے معنی اثبات قوت کے اسلئے ہیں کہ جو آدمی مملوک اور غلام ہو گیا۔ وہ بمنزلہ جمادات کے ہو جاتا ہے اور آزاد کرنے کی وجہ سے زندہ ہو کر قدرت و قوت پاتا ہے اور منکوحہ عورت کا یہ حال نہیں ہے۔ اسلئے کہ وہ ہر تصرف پر قادر ہے ۔ مگر قید نکاحی اس کو تصرف کرنے سے روکتی ہے۔ پس جب اس کو طلاق دے دی۔ تو یہ مانع دور ہو گیا اور مانع دور ہونے سے اس کی قوت ظاہر ہو جائے گی اور یہ بات اظہر من الشمس ہے کہ اعتاق اقوی ہے اور طلاق ادنی ہے اور قاعدہ ہے کہ ادنی کو اعلیٰ کیلئے مستعار نہیں لیا جاتا۔ اسلئے لفظ طلاق مجازاً آزاد کرنے کے معنی میں مستعمل نہیں ہوگا اور یہ بھی دلیل ہے کہ ملک رقبہ بہ نسبت ملک نکاح کے بڑھی ہوئی ہے۔ کیونکہ ملک یمین، ملک نکاح کو مستلزم ہوتا ہے مگر ملک نکاح ملک یمین کو ستلزم نہیں ہوتا۔ پس جب ملک یمین اقومی ہے تو اس کو ساقط کرنا بھی اقومی ہوگا کیونکہ جو چیز قوی ہوتی ہے اس کا اسقاط بھی اقوی ہوتا ہے اور لفظ اپنی حقیقت سے کمتر کیلئے مجاز ہو سکتا ہے۔ مگر اپنی حقیقت سے برتر کیلئے مجاز نہیں ہوسکتا۔ پس ثابت ہو گیا کہ مسئلہ مختلف فیہ میں انت طالق بول کر مجازاً آزادی مراد نہیں لی جاسکتی ہے۔ البتہ اس کا بر عکس جائز ہے۔ یعنی بیوی کو انت حر کہ کر مجاز اطلاق مراد لی جاسکتی ہے۔ واللہ اعلم
Book of Emancipation. Ashraf al-Hidayah Urdu commentary of Hidayah, volume five. His dropping is stronger, and the word can be used metaphorically for what is less than its literal meaning, not for what is above it, so it is prevented in the disputed matter but permitted in its opposite. Translation: If the master said to his slave-girl: 'You are divorced' or 'you are irrevocably divorced' or 'cover your head with a veil' intending thereby emancipation, she does not become free. Imam al-Shafi'i said she becomes free if the master intends it. This same disagreement applies to all words of divorce, explicit and implicit, as the Shafi'i scholars have stated. The proof of Imam al-Shafi'i is that the master intended a meaning which his word can possibly bear because there is an analogy between the two types of ownership: each is an ownership of the essence. For ownership of the slave is clearly ownership of essence, and similarly ownership in marriage is treated as ownership of essence, so much so that marriage requires permanence and fixing a time invalidates it. The action of both words is to cancel one's right, i.e., ownership, and therefore suspending emancipation on a condition is valid. In any case, the rulings are established due to the prior cause, and he is responsible. And since the words 'free' and 'emancipated' become implied words for divorce, the reverse is correct. Our proof is that he intended a meaning which the word cannot bear, because in language 'itq means granting strength and talaq means untying the shackle. This is because the slave is treated like inanimate objects, and by emancipation he becomes alive and gains power and strength. The married woman is not so because she is capable of all actions, but the restriction of marriage prevents her; by divorce the impediment is removed and her power becomes manifest. There is no doubt that the first is stronger, and because ownership by right hand is greater than ownership in marriage, so cancelling it is also stronger. A word can be used metaphorically for something less than its literal meaning, not for something greater. For this reason it is impermissible in the disputed case and permissible in the opposite. Explanation of the issue: If a master says to his slave-girl 'you are divorced' or 'you are irrevocably divorced' or 'cover yourself' meaning with a veil, intending emancipation, she does not become free. Imam al-Shafi'i said she becomes free if he intends it. This disagreement applies to all words of divorce, explicit or implicit. For example, saying to his slave-girl: 'you are divorced', 'I have divorced you', 'get lost', 'you are free', 'you are disengaged', etc. Two narrations from Imam Ahmad: one with the Hanafis and one with the Shafi'is. The proof of Imam al-Shafi'i: The master intended a meaning that his speech can contain, because ownership of the slave and ownership in marriage are mutually analogous. The analogy is that both are ownership of the essence. In ownership of the slave, ownership of essence is apparent, and ownership in marriage is also in the ruling of ownership of essence, so much so that marriage requires the intention of permanence, just as a sale contract requires permanence, and if marriage is for a limited time it is invalid. Thus it is proven that ownership in marriage is legally in the ruling of ownership of essence. 'And the action of both words' is an answer to a question. The question is that emancipation is a name for establishing power in the slave. So emancipation establishes rulings that were not previously established, e.g., after emancipation the slave becomes owner of buying and selling, eligible for testimony and judgeship, and gets rights of guardianship. These are the meanings of legal power. Divorce is pure cancellation: through divorce the husband only cancels his ownership in marriage. So 'itq is granting power, talaq is pure cancellation. Therefore there is no correlation between them, so how can words of divorce be used metaphorically or as implication for emancipation? The response from Imam al-Shafi'i: The action of both words, i.e., the word divorce and the word emancipation, is to cancel one's right, i.e., remove ownership. Just as the word divorce removes ownership in marriage, so the word emancipation removes ownership of the slave. So emancipation is also cancellation like divorce, and because emancipation is cancellation, suspending it on a condition is valid, just as suspending divorce on a condition is valid. Thus there is a correlation between divorce and emancipation. When there is a correlation, words of divorce can be used metaphorically for emancipation. As for the establishment of rulings, that is due to the prior cause: a person being responsible, sane, adult, free. Rulings are established due to humanity, but slavery was an impediment to rulings. When the impediment is removed by emancipation, rulings are established due to the prior cause, i.e., humanity. So it is proven that emancipation is not granting power but cancellation like divorce. So since there is correlation, just as it is valid to use words of emancipation metaphorically for divorce, similarly it is valid to use words of divorce for emancipation. Just as if a man says to his wife 'you are free' intending divorce, divorce occurs, so if a man says to his slave-girl 'you are divorced' intending emancipation, she becomes free. Our proof is that the master, by saying 'you are divorced' or 'irrevocably divorced' or other words of divorce, intends a meaning that the word cannot possibly bear. There is no correlation between divorce and emancipation, because in language 'itq means giving strength, talaq means removing the marriage tie. Emancipation means granting power because the person enslaved becomes like inanimate objects, and by emancipation he becomes alive and gains power. The married woman is not like that because she is capable of all actions, but the marriage tie prevents her. When he divorces her, the impediment is removed and her power becomes manifest. It is clearer than the sun that emancipation is stronger and divorce is weaker. The rule is that something weaker cannot be borrowed for something stronger, so the word divorce cannot be used metaphorically for emancipation. Another proof: ownership of the slave is superior to ownership in marriage, because ownership by right hand entails ownership in marriage, but ownership in marriage does not entail ownership by right hand. So when ownership by right hand is stronger, its cancellation is also stronger. A word can be used metaphorically for something less than its literal meaning, but not for something greater. So it is proven that in this disputed matter, saying 'you are divorced' cannot metaphorically mean emancipation. However, the opposite is permissible: saying to the wife 'you are free' can metaphorically mean divorce. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage addresses a dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i regarding the use of divorce words (like 'you are divorced') for the purpose of emancipating a slave-girl. The Hanafi position is that such words do not effect emancipation even if the master so intends, whereas Imam al-Shafi'i holds that emancipation takes effect if intention is present. The passage first states the ruling and then presents both sides' proofs and rebuttals. The Shafi'i argument relies on the analogy (qiyas) between ownership of a slave (mulk yamin) and ownership in marriage (mulk nikah): both are forms of ownership of the essence, and the effect of both divorce and emancipation is the cancellation of that ownership. Therefore, words of one can be used metaphorically for the other. The Hanafi response counters that there is a qualitative difference: emancipation (itq) grants power and life to the slave (like a resurrection from inanimate status), while divorce merely removes a restriction from a woman who already possesses full capacity. Hence 'itq is stronger, and weaker words cannot be used for a stronger meaning. Additionally, mulk yamin is a stronger ownership (it encompasses the slave's entire being and implies mulk nikah with a slave-girl), while mulk nikah is limited. Thus words of talaq (weaker) cannot be used for 'itq (stronger) metaphorically. However, the reverse is allowed: if one says to his wife 'you are free' intending divorce, it counts as a valid divorce. The passage concludes with 'wa Allahu a'lam'.

Key Terms
ملک یمین (mulk yamin): Ownership by right hand, i.e., ownership of a slave; it is total ownership of the slave's person and entails ownership in marriage if the slave is female.
ملک نکاح (mulk nikah): Ownership in marriage, i.e., the right of the husband over the wife established through the marriage contract, which is terminable by divorce.
اعتاق (i'taq (or itq)): Emancipation of a slave, which according to Hanafis grants the slave legal capacity and powers (e.g., ownership, testimony) by removing the impediment of slavery.
طلاق (talaq): Divorce, the dissolution of the marriage contract, which cancels the husband's ownership (mulk nikah) over the wife.
اقوی (aqwa): Stronger; used here to compare 'itq (stronger) and talaq (weaker) based on the entailment relationship and effect on the subject.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school as explained in the passage, if a master says to his slave-girl 'you are divorced' intending emancipation, what is the ruling?
A) She becomes free because the intention is valid.
B) She does not become free because divorce words cannot be used for emancipation.
C) She becomes free only if he says 'Taliq' and not other words.
D) The ruling depends on whether he said 'anti taliq' or 'anti bain'.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that words of divorce cannot effect emancipation because emancipation (itq) is a stronger act of granting power, and weak words cannot stand for a strong meaning.

Talaq — Divorce

Hanafi Response to Shafi'i on Metaphorical Use of Talaq for Itq
Debate whether talaq words can metaphorically mean emancipation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school rejects the metaphorical use of talaq for itq and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
A word can be metaphorically used only for something weaker, not stronger; emancipation is stronger than divorce, so talaq cannot mean itq.

In this passage, the author presents a key dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools: can the words of divorce (talaq) be used metaphorically to mean emancipation (itq)? Both schools agree that words like 'free' (hurr) can metaphorically mean divorce when used for a wife. But the reverse — using 'divorce' for freeing a slave — is contested. The Hanafi school argues this is impossible because emancipation establishes legal power (quwwa) while divorce only removes a marital bond; the weaker cannot be borrowed for the stronger. We will follow the arguments and understand the practical consequence of this difference.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the Hanafi ruling on using talaq words for emancipation. According to the text, it is not valid; the words of divorce cannot be used to free a slave.
The Hanafi position is that the slave remains enslaved despite the master's intention.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that such use is valid because both talaq and itq involve removal of ownership (izalat al-milk).
Shafi'i considers the shared element of isqat sufficient for metaphorical transfer.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Examine the Shafi'i argument: Both words effect removal of a right (ownership). Just as talaq removes milk nikah, itq removes milk riqq. Hence there is munasaba (suitability).
The Shafi'i also argue that suspending emancipation on a condition is analogous to suspending divorce.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response distinguishes the nature of the removal: itq establishes power (quwwa) in the slave, while talaq merely removes a bond. Emancipation is stronger (aqwa) because it grants full legal capacity; divorce is weaker (adna). A weaker word cannot be used metaphorically for a stronger one.
The text also highlights that milk yamin (slave ownership) is stronger than milk nikah (marriage ownership), so its removal is also stronger.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: If a master says 'anti taliq' intending emancipation, Hanafi say she is not freed, Shafi'i say she is freed. Conversely, using 'anti hurrah' for divorce is valid in both schools.
This illustrates the asymmetry based on strength of the two acts.

Book of Emancipation. Page 294. Ashraf al-Bidaya Sharh Urdu Hidaya vol 2 (complete). So that through emancipation those rulings become established which were not established before emancipation. For example, after freedom the slave becomes owner of sale and purchase, becomes qualified for testimony and judgeship, and obtains the right of guardianship. And these are the meanings of legal power (quwwa shar'iyya). And divorce (talaq) is the name of mere removal (isqat mahd). That is, through divorce only the husband's ownership of the marriage bond (milk nikah) falls away. The conclusion is that emancipation (itq) is the name of establishing power (ithbat quwwa) and divorce is mere removal. Due to this, there is no suitability (munasaba) between the two. Therefore, how can it be correct to use the words of divorce metaphorically (majazan) and as allusion (kinaya) for emancipation? The response from Imam Shafi'i is that the action of both words, i.e., the word divorce and the word emancipation, is that they remove a right, i.e., remove ownership. Just as the word divorce removes the ownership of marriage, similarly the word emancipation removes the ownership of slaves. The conclusion is that emancipation is also removal like divorce. And since emancipation is removal, therefore, it is correct to suspend emancipation on a condition, just as it is correct to suspend divorce on a condition. So the summary is that there is suitability between divorce and emancipation, and when suitability exists, the words of divorce can be used metaphorically in the meaning of freeing. And the establishment of other rulings occurs with the previous cause, i.e., a person being legally responsible, sane, adult, free. That is, the establishment of rulings is due to humanity. But slavery was an obstacle for the establishment of rulings. So when the obstacle is removed through emancipation, the rulings will be established due to the previous cause, i.e., humanity. So it is established that emancipation is not the name of establishing power, but like divorce, it is the name of removal. Therefore, since there is suitability between the words of divorce and the words of emancipation, just as it is metaphorically correct to intend divorce by the words of emancipation, similarly it will also be correct to intend freedom by the words of divorce. That is, just as when a man says to his wife 'You are free' and intends divorce, divorce occurs, similarly if a person says to his slave girl 'You are divorced' and intends to free her, she becomes free. And our proof is that the master intends meanings from the words 'You are divorced' or 'You are bain' or other words of divorce that these words do not have the probability for. That is, there is no suitability between divorce and emancipation. Because lexically, emancipation (itq) means to give power (quwwa) and divorce means to remove the marital bond (qaid nikahi). And the meaning of emancipation as establishment of power is because a person who becomes owned and a slave becomes like inanimate objects, and through being freed he becomes alive and attains power and strength. And the married woman is not like that, because she is capable of every transaction, but the marital bond prevents her from transacting. So when she is divorced, this obstacle is removed, and with the removal of the obstacle, her power becomes apparent. And it is clearer than the sun that emancipation is stronger (aqwa) and divorce is weaker (adna). And the rule is that the weaker is not borrowed (musta'ar) for the stronger. Therefore, the word divorce will not be used metaphorically in the meaning of freeing. And this is also a proof that the ownership of slaves (milk riqq) is greater than the ownership of marriage (milk nikah). Because the ownership of the right hand (milk yamin) entails the ownership of marriage, but the ownership of marriage does not entail the ownership of the right hand. So when the ownership of the right hand is stronger, its removal will also be stronger, because whatever is strong, its removal is also stronger. And a word can be metaphorical for something less than its reality, but cannot be metaphorical for something greater than its reality. So it is established that in the disputed case, by saying 'You are divorced' one cannot metaphorically intend freedom. However, the reverse is permissible, i.e., by saying 'You are free' to the wife, one can metaphorically intend divorce. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding whether the words of divorce (talaq) can be used metaphorically to mean emancipation (itq). The Hanafi position, as argued by the commentator, is that this is not permissible because the two acts are fundamentally different in nature. The author first presents the Hanafi view: emancipation establishes legal power (quwwa) in the slave, who was previously like an inanimate object, while divorce merely removes the marital bond from a woman who already has legal capacity. Thus there is no natural linguistic suitability (munasaba) between the two. Then the author presents the Shafi'i counter-argument: both talaq and itq involve the removal of ownership (izalat al-milk), so they share the common meaning of isqat (removal). Hence they are suitable for metaphorical transfer. The Hanafi response refutes this by distinguishing the strength of the two removals. Emancipation removes the stronger ownership (milk yamin), which entails everything, while divorce removes the weaker ownership (milk nikah). Since a word can only be metaphorically extended to something lesser, not greater, talaq cannot be used for itq. However, the reverse is allowed: using itq words for talaq, as explained in the text. The passage concludes with the established ruling that if a master says 'anti taliq' to his slave-girl intending emancipation, she does not become free; but if he says 'anti hurrah' to his wife intending divorce, divorce occurs.

Key Terms
مناسبت (munasaba): Linguistic suitability or common ground between two words that allows metaphorical transfer.
قوت شرعیہ (quwwa shar'iyya): Legal capacity to own property, give testimony, act as judge, etc.
ملک یمین (milk yamin): Ownership of a slave (right hand), considered stronger than milk nikah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that talaq words cannot metaphorically mean emancipation because emancipation establishes power (aqwa) while divorce removes a bond (adna), and a weaker word cannot be borrowed for a stronger meaning.

Talaq — Divorce

Partial Emancipation: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on partial emancipation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on partial emancipation, explain their proofs, and identify the mufta bihi position with its practical consequence.

Core Idea
Can you free half a slave? Abu Hanifa says yes; the Sahibayn say no – the whole must go free.

This passage from al-Hidayah presents a classic intra-Hanafi dispute on whether emancipation ('itq) is divisible. The core question: if a master frees a portion of his slave, does only that portion become free, or does the entire slave become free? Imam Abu Hanifa and the two disciples (Sahibayn) – Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani – differ fundamentally on this, each appealing to different conceptions of legal capacity and property rights.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the dispute
The act is partial emancipation (i'taq ba'd al-'abd). The dispute is whether such an act results in freedom of only that portion or the entire slave.
The act itself is valid in both views, but the effect differs.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and proof
Abu Hanifa: emancipation removes ownership (milk), which is divisible (like in sale). The freed portion is free; the rest remains enslaved, and the slave must earn (sa'y) the value of that remaining portion to compensate the master.
He draws on the principle that disposition is limited to the authority of the disposer, and ownership is divisible.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and proof
The Sahibayn: emancipation establishes legal capacity (qutba hukmiyya), which is indivisible (like divorce or forgiveness of qisas). Freeing any part necessarily frees the whole because slavery (riqq) and legal capacity cannot be partial.
They analogize to acts that are indivisible by consensus: talaq, 'afw min al-qisas, and istilad.
4
Analyze Abu Hanifa's reconciliation
Abu Hanifa responds by making the partially freed slave a mukatab (one who earns to free himself). This satisfies both proofs: the master's ownership over the unfreed portion is retained, but the slave has an opportunity to complete his freedom through earnings. If unable, he is not returned to full slavery (unlike kitaba).
This intermediate solution respects divisibility of ownership while ensuring the slave is not permanently partially enslaved.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and practical consequence
The later relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that of the Sahibayn: full immediate freedom for the slave. This is more cautious and avoids long-term partial slavery. The practical difference: under Abu Hanifa the slave works to pay off the remaining value; under Sahibayn he is instantly free, and the master must seek compensation through earnings or other means.
This affects inheritance, marriage, and other legal capacities.

Source Text
